Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
Distinguishing Conception and Assent
Author defines the two main divisions of mental perceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq by identifying the presence or absence of a judgment in a given mental perception.

Core Idea
Every mental perception is either a bare concept or a judgment on a relation.

This passage establishes the foundational division in Islamic epistemology: all that the mind grasps is either a simple concept or a judgment. Understanding this distinction is critical because every subsequent logical operation — definition, syllogism, proof — builds on either tasawwur or tasdiq. The author defines each and explains the role of innate mental faculties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Notice that the passage divides all mental perceptions into two and gives them names: tasawwurāt and tasdīqāt.
2
Extract the defining feature of tasawwur
Tasawwur is the apprehension of a single individual thing or meaning without any judgment about relations.
The mind simply has the form of the concept, without affirming or denying anything.
3
Extract the defining feature of tasdiq
Tasdiq is the apprehension of a relation (affirmative or negative) between two or more concepts.
This happens even if the judgment is mistaken or uncertain.
4
Check the scope of tasdiq
Note that tasdiq does not require correctness or certainty; any judgment that affirms or negates a relation is a tasdiq.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a mental content, decide if it is a tasawwur (no judgment) or a tasdiq (involves a judgment of relation).
Practice with examples from Islamic sciences, e.g. the concept 'wajib' vs. the statement 'wajib is commanded by Allah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه المدركات التي ترد إلى أذهاننا من هذه الطرق تكون بمثابة المواد الخام للأعمال الفكرية الاستنتاجية، إذ يعمل الفكر على استنتاج مدركات جديدة لم ترد إليه عن أي من الطرق الثلاثة الآنفة الذكر، منها الاستنتاجات الرياضية والاستدلالات العقلية المختلفة، كاستنتاج العقل وجود المؤثر عند ملاحظة الأثر، واستنباط المعاني المجرَّدة من ظواهر الأشياء المدركة بالحس، وتتم الاستنتاجات والاستنباطات العقلية بملاحظة الذهن للمدركات الواردة إليه من الخارج، وذلك بما منحه الله من استعداد فطري للاستنتاج، وللاستنباط، وبما وضع فيه من قدرة يستطيع بها تمييز الحقائق، وتحليل المركبات إلى عناصرها، وتركيب العناصر في صورة منسجمة، واستخلاص المعاني الكلية من الجزئيات المتشابهة، كما وضع في الحواس قدرات تستطيع بها تمييز صفات الأشياء، ولولا هذه القدرات الفطرية لاختلت موازين المدركات سواء أكانت مدركات حِسِّيَّةً أو مدركات عقلية. وحينما نراقب ما في أذهاننا من مدركات مختلفة واردة إليها من العالم الخارج عنها، أو حاصلة فيها مما استنتجته قدرات الاستنتاج فيها بالنظر فيما ورد إليها من الخارج؛ فإننا نستطيع تقسيمها إلى قسمين رئيسين: أ ـ فهي إما أن تكون مجرد مفردات منبثة تقع صورتها في الذهن، دون أن يحكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، ولنسم هذه (تصورات) فهذا هو القسم الأول وهو (التصورات). ب ـ وإما أن تكون هذه المفردات الواقعة صورتها في الذهن قد حكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، سواء أكان الذهن مصيباً في حكمه أو مخطئاً، وسواء أوصل إلى درجة الجزم أو لم يصل، ولنسم هذه (تصديقات) وهذا هو القسم الثاني وهو (التصديقات). تعريف التصور والتصديق: فالتصور: هو إدراك أي مفرد من مفردات الأشياء والمعاني. والتّصديق: هو إدراك النسبة بين مفردين فأكثر، وهذه النسبة إما موجبة وإما سالبة، أي: إما مثبتة وإما منفية.
These perceptions that come to our minds through these paths are like raw material for intellectual deductive operations, for the intellect works to deduce new perceptions that have not come to it from any of the three aforementioned paths. Among them are mathematical deductions and various rational inferences, such as the mind's inference of the existence of an effect upon observing the cause, and the extraction of abstract meanings from the phenomena of things perceived by sense. And rational deductions and inferences are accomplished by the mind's observation of the perceptions coming to it from outside, by virtue of what God has granted it of innate readiness for deduction and inference, and by virtue of the ability placed in it to distinguish truths, analyze compounds into their elements, synthesize elements into a coherent form, and extract universal meanings from similar particulars, just as He placed in the senses abilities to distinguish the qualities of things. Were it not for these innate abilities, the balances of perceptions would be disturbed, whether they are sensory perceptions or rational perceptions. And when we observe what is in our minds of various perceptions coming from the external world, or obtained from what the deductive abilities have deduced by considering what has come from outside, we can divide them into two main divisions: A) Either they are mere scattered individuals whose form occurs in the mind without the mind judging to affirm or negate relations between them — let us call these 'conceptions' (taṣawwurāt), and this is the first division. B) Or these individuals whose form occurs in the mind have been judged by the mind by affirming or negating relations between them, whether the mind is correct in its judgment or mistaken, and whether it reaches certainty or not — let us call these 'assents' (taṣdīqāt), and this is the second division. Definition of conception and assent: Conception is the apprehension of any single individual of things and meanings. Assent is the apprehension of the relation between two or more individuals, and this relation is either affirmative or negative, i.e., either positive or negative.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental categories of mental perceptions: conceptions (taṣawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt). The author begins by reminding that the three earlier paths provide raw material for intellectual operations. The mind then uses innate abilities to deduce new perceptions, analyze, and synthesize. After describing these mental faculties, the author presents the classification: perceptions are either simple concepts without judgment (conceptions) or judgments that affirm or deny relations (assents). Finally, explicit definitions are given: conception is the apprehension of a single thing or meaning; assent is the apprehension of a relation between two or more things, either positive or negative. This division is foundational in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes 'tasdiq' as defined in the passage?
A) A simple concept in the mind without any relation
B) The apprehension of a relation between two or more concepts, either positive or negative
C) The innate ability to deduce new knowledge
D) The sensory perception of external objects

Key Takeaway
The division into conceptions and assents is the starting point for all logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Identify which of the following is a tasawwur and which a tasdiq: (a) 'human' (b) 'human is rational' (c) 'the sky is blue'. Explain your reasoning based on the definitions in the passage.
?
Task 2: Think of a concept in Islamic theology, such as 'iman' (faith). Formulate a tasawwur of 'iman' (a simple mental concept) and then a tasdiq about it (e.g., 'iman increases and decreases'). Explain the difference in terms of the presence or absence of a judgment.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
The Two Divisions of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author defines tasawwur and tasdiq and illustrates with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) using the criterion of whether a judgment of relation is present.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either a concept or an assent; the difference is judgment.

The passage introduces the foundational division of all mental perceptions. Before we can evaluate arguments, we must understand the two kinds of knowledge they produce. This lesson will equip you to identify whether a given piece of knowledge is a concept or an assent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presence of judgment
Look at the mental content. Is there a relation (nisba) being affirmed or denied? If yes, it is tasdiq; if no, it is tasawwur.
The passage clarifies that tasdiq involves a judgment that could be correct or mistaken.
2
Check for singulars vs. related singulars
Tasawwur consists of singular forms without connection. Tasdiq links at least two singulars with a relation.
Examples: 'rock' and 'hardness' are concepts; 'rock is hard' is an assent.
3
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the concept of 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'prayer'. 'Wajib' alone is a concept. 'Prayer is wajib' is an assent.
The same singulars can appear in both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرياضية والاستدلالات العقلية المختلفة، كاستنتاج العقل وجود المؤثر عند ملاحظة الأثر، واستنباط المعاني المجرَّدة من ظواهر الأشياء المدركة بالحس، وتتم الاستنتاجات والاستنباطات العقلية بملاحظة الذهن للمدركات الواردة إليه من الخارج، وذلك بما منحه الله من استعداد فطري للاستنتاج، وللاستنباط، وبما وضع فيه من قدرة يستطيع بها تمييز الحقائق، وتحليل المركبات إلى عناصرها، وتركيب العناصر في صورة منسجمة، واستخلاص المعاني الكلية من الجزئيات المتشابهة، كما وضع في الحواس قدرات تستطيع بها تمييز صفات الأشياء، ولولا هذه القدرات الفطرية لاختلت موازين المدركات سواء أكانت مدركات حِسِّيَّةً أو مدركات عقلية.
وحينما نراقب ما في أذهاننا من مدركات مختلفة واردة إليها من العالم الخارج عنها، أو حاصلة فيها مما استنتجته قدرات الاستنتاج فيها بالنظر فيما ورد إليها من الخارج؛ فإننا نستطيع تقسيمها إلى قسمين رئيسين:
أ ـ فهي إما أن تكون مجرد مفردات منبثة تقع صورت haunt في الذهن، دون أن يحكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، ولنسم هذه (تصورات) فهذا هو القسم الأول وهو (التصورات).
ب ـ وإما أن تكون هذه المفردات الواقعة صورتها في الذهن قد حكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، سواء أكان الذهن مصيباً في حكمه أو مخطئاً، وسواء أوصل إلى درجة الجزم أو لم يصل، ولنسم هذه (تصديقات) وهذا هو القسم الثاني وهو (التصديقات).
تعريف التصور والتصديق:
فالتصور: هو إدراك أي مفرد من مفردات الأشياء والمعاني.
والتصديق: هو إدراك النسبة بين مفردين فأكثر، وهذه النسبة إما موجبة وإما سالبة، أي: إما مثبتة وإما منفية.
ولدى التأمل نلاحظ أن أول ما يقع في أذهاننا من المعارف إنما هو صور مفردات الأشياء والمعاني، ثم نربط بين هذه المفردات التي وقعت صورها في أذهاننا بنسب ما، وهذه النسب (أي العلاقات) إما نسب موجبة، وإما نسب سالبة، فإذا أدركنا هذه النِّسب فقد أصدرنا حكمنا الفكري.
وهنا يتضح لنا أنه ما دمنا في حدود إدراك المفردات المنبثة، دون أن ندرك في الذهن علاقات بينها فإننا لا نزال في حدود الإدراك التصوري، أما إذا أدركنا علاقة ما (نسبة) فإننا حينئذ ننتقل من الإدراك التصوري الذي كان وحده، إلى الإدراك التصديقي معه، وكل إدراك تصديقي لا ينفك عن إدراكات تصورية في ضمنه سابقة له في حصول الإدراك.
أمثلة:
أ ـ فمن صور المفردات التي تقع في أذهاننا صورة الصخر وصورة القسوة، ثم ندرك أن بين الصخر والقسوة علاقة موصوف بصفة، هذه العلاقة هي النسبة التي أدركناها، فإذا قررنا أن الصخر له صفة القسوة فقد أصدرنا حكماً إيجابياً بذلك، وهذا الحكم هو حكم تصديقي جاء قبله تصورات غير مقترنة بحكم.
ب ـ ومن صور المفردات التي تقع في أذهاننا صورة الثلج وصورة الحرارة، إن إدراكنا هذا لا يزال في حدود التصور، فإذا أدركنا أن بين الثلج والحرارة نسبة سالبة، فقد أصدرنا حكمنا الفكري بأن الثلج ليس حاراً، إنّ إدراكنا لهذه النسبة السالبة إدراك تصديقي جاء قبله تصورات غير مقترنة بحكم.
وهكذا نستطيع أن نقيس على هذين المثالين كل الإدراكات الذهنية، تصوراتها وتصديقاتها.
ساحة المدركات الذهنية
طعام حجر
ثلج قاسي جميل
حار سماء وجه عمل
بارد نار قبيح طيب
هذه مفردات تصورية
طعام طيب ، الثلج ليس حاراً
السماء باردة ، وجه جميل
عمل غير حسن، حجر قاسي
هذه الجمل تتضمن نسباً تصديقية
Mathematical and various intellectual deductions, such as the intellect inferring the existence of an effect when observing the cause, and deriving abstract meanings from the appearances of things perceived by sense. Intellectual deductions and inferences are accomplished by the mind observing perceptions received from outside, through what God has granted it of innate readiness for deduction and inference, and through the capacity placed in it by which it can distinguish realities, analyze compounds into their elements, synthesize elements into a coherent form, and extract universal meanings from similar particulars, just as capacities were placed in the senses by which they can distinguish the attributes of things. Were it not for these innate capacities, the scales of perceptions—whether sensory perceptions or intellectual perceptions—would be imbalanced.
When we observe what is in our minds of various perceptions coming from the external world, or resulting from what the inferential capacities have deduced from what came from outside, we can divide them into two main divisions:
A) Either they are mere scattered singulars whose form occurs in the mind without the mind judging affirmation or negation of relations among them—let us call these 'concepts' (tasawwurāt). This is the first division: concepts.
B) Or these singulars whose forms occur in the mind have been judged by the mind with affirmation or negation of relations among them, whether the mind is correct in its judgment or mistaken, whether it reaches certainty or not—let us call these 'assents' (tasdīqāt). This is the second division: assents.
Definition of Concept and Assent:
Concept (tasawwur): It is the apprehension of any singular among the singulars of things and meanings.
Assent (tasdīq): It is the apprehension of the relation (nisba) between two or more singulars. This relation is either affirmative or negative, i.e., either positive or negative.
Upon reflection, we observe that the first knowledge that occurs in our minds is only forms of singulars of things and meanings; then we connect these singulars, whose forms have occurred in our minds, with certain relations. These relations (i.e., connections) are either affirmative relations or negative relations. When we apprehend these relations, we have issued our intellectual judgment.
Here it becomes clear to us that as long as we are within the limits of apprehending scattered singulars without perceiving relations among them in the mind, we remain within the limits of conceptual apprehension. But if we perceive a relation (nisba), then we move from conceptual apprehension alone to assent apprehension along with it. Every assent apprehension is inseparable from conceptual apprehensions that are implicit within it and precede it in obtaining apprehension.
Examples:
A) Among the forms of singulars that occur in our minds are the form of 'rock' and the form of 'hardness'. Then we perceive a relation of being described by a quality between rock and hardness. This relation is the relation we apprehend. If we decide that rock has the quality of hardness, we have issued an affirmative judgment to that effect. This judgment is an assent judgment preceded by concepts not combined with a judgment.
B) Among the forms of singulars that occur in our minds are the form of 'snow' and the form of 'heat'. This apprehension of ours remains within the limits of concept. If we perceive a negative relation between snow and heat, we have issued our intellectual judgment that snow is not hot. Our apprehension of this negative relation is an assent apprehension preceded by concepts not combined with a judgment.
Thus we can analogize all mental apprehensions—their concepts and assents—to these two examples.
The Field of Mental Perceptions:
Food, Stone
Snow, Hard, Beautiful
Hot, Sky, Face, Work
Cold, Fire, Ugly, Good
These are conceptual singulars.
Food is good; Snow is not hot.
Sky is cold; Face is beautiful.
Work is not good; Stone is hard.
These sentences contain relational assents.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage serves as an epistemological introduction to the fundamental division of knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by describing the innate mental faculties that enable deduction, analysis, and synthesis, emphasizing that without these God-given capacities, perceptions would be imbalanced. Then, focusing on the content of the mind, the author divides all mental perceptions into two kinds: scattered singulars without judgment (tasawwurāt) and singulars combined with a judgment of relation (tasdīqāt). The definitions are then given: tasawwur is the apprehension of a singular meaning, while tasdiq is the apprehension of a relation (affirmative or negative) between two or more singulars. The author clarifies that the first step in knowledge is the reception of singular concepts; then the mind connects them with affirmative or negative relations, thereby issuing a judgment. Importantly, every tasdiq includes implicit tasawwurāt that precede it. Examples illustrate: the concept of 'rock' and 'hardness' are separate until we judge 'rock is hard' (affirmative tasdiq); similarly, 'snow' and 'heat' are concepts, but 'snow is not hot' is a negative tasdiq. The diagram at the end, titled 'The Field of Mental Perceptions', provides a visual arrangement: the left column lists conceptual singulars (e.g., food, stone, snow, etc.) and the right column gives example sentences containing relational judgments (e.g., 'food is good', 'snow is not hot'). This reinforces the division and shows how the same singulars appear in both conceptual and judgmental contexts. The passage thus establishes the foundational epistemological framework used in logic for analyzing knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes tasawwur from tasdiq?
A) Tasawwur deals with composite ideas, while tasdiq deals with simple ones.
B) Tasawwur involves apprehension of singulars without judgment, while tasdiq involves judgment of a relation.
C) Tasawwur is always correct, while tasdiq can be mistaken.
D) Tasawwur comes after tasdiq in the order of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of knowledge is between concepts (tasawwur) without judgment and assents (tasdiq) with judgment of a relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a concept or an assent: (a) 'the concept of Allah's existence', (b) 'Allah exists', (c) 'the concept of justice', (d) 'justice is commanded by Allah'.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
Definitions of Tasawwur and Tasdiq: Grasping Singulars and Relations
Author defines tasawwur and tasdiq explicitly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tasawwur and tasdiq precisely according to the author and articulate the role of the relation (nisba).

Core Idea
Tasawwur is apprehension of a singular; tasdiq is apprehension of a relation between singulars.

The passage provides explicit definitions of the two terms. Understanding these definitions is crucial because they form the basis for all logical analysis. This lesson will break down the components of each definition.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition of tasawwur
Apprehension of any singular of things and meanings. It is a simple act of perception without any relation.
The term 'idrak' is used for both.
2
Parse the definition of tasdiq
Apprehension of the relation (nisba) between two or more singulars, affirmative or negative.
The relation is either positive or negative, and the judgment may be correct or mistaken.
3
Examine the role of nisba
The nisba is the core of tasdiq; without it, we remain in tasawwur. The nisba is a mental relation.
Example: 'rock is hard' contains nisba of affirmation; 'snow is not hot' contains nisba of negation.

والتصديق: هو إدراك النسبة بين مفردين فأكثر، وهذه النسبة إما موجبة وإما سالبة، أي: إما مثبتة وإما منفية.
ولدى التأمل نلاحظ أن أول ما يقع في أذهاننا من المعارف إنما هو صور مفردات الأشياء والمعاني، ثم نربط بين هذه المفردات التي وقعت صورها في أذهاننا بنسب ما، وهذه النسب (أي العلاقات) إما نسب موجبة، وإما نسب سالبة، فإذا أدركنا هذه النِّسب فقد أصدرنا حكمنا الفكري.
وهنا يتضح لنا أنه ما دمنا في حدود إدراك المفردات المنبثة، دون أن ندرك في الذهن علاقات بينها فإننا لا نزال في حدود الإدراك التصوري، أما إذا أدركنا علاقة ما (نسبة) فإننا حينئذ ننتقل من الإدراك التصوري الذي كان وحده، إلى الإدراك التصديقي معه، وكل إدراك تصديقي لا ينفك عن إدراكات تصورية في ضمنه سابقة له في حصول الإدراك.
أمثلة:
أ ـ فمن صور المفردات التي تقع في أذهاننا صورة الصخر وصورة القسوة، ثم ندرك أن بين الصخر والقسوة علاقة موصوف بصفة، هذه العلاقة هي النسبة التي أدركناها، فإذا قررنا أن الصخر له صفة القسوة فقد أصدرنا حكماً إيجابياً بذلك، وهذا الحكم هو حكم تصديقي جاء قبله تصورات غير مقترنة بحكم.
ب ـ ومن صور المفردات التي تقع في أذهاننا صورة الثلج وصورة الحرارة، إن إدراكنا هذا لا يزال في حدود التصور، فإذا أدركنا أن بين الثلج والحرارة نسبة سالبة، فقد أصدرنا حكمنا الفكري بأن الثلج ليس حاراً، إنّ إدراكنا لهذه النسبة السالبة إدراك تصديقي جاء قبله تصورات غير مقترنة بحكم.
وهكذا نستطيع أن نقيس على هذين المثالين كل الإدراكات الذهنية، تصوراتها وتصديقاتها.
ساحة المدركات الذهنية
طعام حجر
ثلج قاسي جميل
حار سماء وجه عمل
بارد نار قبيح طيب
هذه مفردات تصورية
طعام طيب ، الثلج ليس حاراً
السماء باردة ، وجه جميل
عمل غير حسن، حجر قاسي
هذه الجمل تتضمن نسباً تصديقية
Mathematical and various intellectual deductions, such as the intellect inferring the existence of an effect when observing the cause, and deriving abstract meanings from the appearances of things perceived by sense. Intellectual deductions and inferences are accomplished by the mind observing perceptions received from outside, through what God has granted it of innate readiness for deduction and inference, and through the capacity placed in it by which it can distinguish realities, analyze compounds into their elements, synthesize elements into a coherent form, and extract universal meanings from similar particulars, just as capacities were placed in the senses by which they can distinguish the attributes of things. Were it not for these innate capacities, the scales of perceptions—whether sensory perceptions or intellectual perceptions—would be imbalanced.
When we observe what is in our minds of various perceptions coming from the external world, or resulting from what the inferential capacities have deduced from what came from outside, we can divide them into two main divisions:
A) Either they are mere scattered singulars whose form occurs in the mind without the mind judging affirmation or negation of relations among them—let us call these 'concepts' (tasawwurāt). This is the first division: concepts.
B) Or these singulars whose forms occur in the mind have been judged by the mind with affirmation or negation of relations among them, whether the mind is correct in its judgment or mistaken, whether it reaches certainty or not—let us call these 'assents' (tasdīqāt). This is the second division: assents.
Definition of Concept and Assent:
Concept (tasawwur): It is the apprehension of any singular among the singulars of things and meanings.
Assent (tasdīq): It is the apprehension of the relation (nisba) between two or more singulars. This relation is either affirmative or negative, i.e., either positive or negative.
Upon reflection, we observe that the first knowledge that occurs in our minds is only forms of singulars of things and meanings; then we connect these singulars, whose forms have occurred in our minds, with certain relations. These relations (i.e., connections) are either affirmative relations or negative relations. When we apprehend these relations, we have issued our intellectual judgment.
Here it becomes clear to us that as long as we are within the limits of apprehending scattered singulars without perceiving relations among them in the mind, we remain within the limits of conceptual apprehension. But if we perceive a relation (nisba), then we move from conceptual apprehension alone to assent apprehension along with it. Every assent apprehension is inseparable from conceptual apprehensions that are implicit within it and precede it in obtaining apprehension.
Examples:
A) Among the forms of singulars that occur in our minds are the form of 'rock' and the form of 'hardness'. Then we perceive a relation of being described by a quality between rock and hardness. This relation is the relation we apprehend. If we decide that rock has the quality of hardness, we have issued an affirmative judgment to that effect. This judgment is an assent judgment preceded by concepts not combined with a judgment.
B) Among the forms of singulars that occur in our minds are the form of 'snow' and the form of 'heat'. This apprehension of ours remains within the limits of concept. If we perceive a negative relation between snow and heat, we have issued our intellectual judgment that snow is not hot. Our apprehension of this negative relation is an assent apprehension preceded by concepts not combined with a judgment.
Thus we can analogize all mental apprehensions—their concepts and assents—to these two examples.
The Field of Mental Perceptions:
Food, Stone
Snow, Hard, Beautiful
Hot, Sky, Face, Work
Cold, Fire, Ugly, Good
These are conceptual singulars.
Food is good; Snow is not hot.
Sky is cold; Face is beautiful.
Work is not good; Stone is hard.
These sentences contain relational assents.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage serves as an epistemological introduction to the fundamental division of knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by describing the innate mental faculties that enable deduction, analysis, and synthesis, emphasizing that without these God-given capacities, perceptions would be imbalanced. Then, focusing on the content of the mind, the author divides all mental perceptions into two kinds: scattered singulars without judgment (tasawwurāt) and singulars combined with a judgment of relation (tasdīqāt). The definitions are then given: tasawwur is the apprehension of a singular meaning, while tasdiq is the apprehension of a relation (affirmative or negative) between two or more singulars. The author clarifies that the first step in knowledge is the reception of singular concepts; then the mind connects them with affirmative or negative relations, thereby issuing a judgment. Importantly, every tasdiq includes implicit tasawwurāt that precede it. Examples illustrate: the concept of 'rock' and 'hardness' are separate until we judge 'rock is hard' (affirmative tasdiq); similarly, 'snow' and 'heat' are concepts, but 'snow is not hot' is a negative tasdiq. The diagram at the end, titled 'The Field of Mental Perceptions', provides a visual arrangement: the left column lists conceptual singulars (e.g., food, stone, snow, etc.) and the right column gives example sentences containing relational judgments (e.g., 'food is good', 'snow is not hot'). This reinforces the division and shows how the same singulars appear in both conceptual and judgmental contexts. The passage thus establishes the foundational epistemological framework used in logic for analyzing knowledge.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
إدراك
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the object of apprehension in tasdiq?
A) A singular meaning.
B) A relation (nisba) between singulars.
C) The correctness of the judgment.
D) The external object itself.

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur is simple apprehension of a singular; tasdiq is apprehension of an affirmative or negative relation between singulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'Zakat is obligatory in Islam.' Identify the singulars (concepts) involved and the nisba (relation) that makes it an assent.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
The Priority of Concepts: How Assents Depend on Concepts
Author argues that concepts precede assents in the order of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why tasawwur precedes tasdiq in the order of knowledge and identify the implicit concepts in any given assent.

Core Idea
Every assent presupposes concepts; you cannot affirm a relation without first having the terms.

The passage emphasizes that the first knowledge to occur in the mind is concepts, and then the mind links them. This lesson explores the priority and the interdependence of the two divisions.

Guided Path
1
Observe the order in the passage
The author says 'first what occurs in our minds are forms of singulars, then we connect them with relations.'
This is a logical, not temporal, priority.
2
Deconstruct an example
Take 'fire is hot'. The concepts 'fire' and 'hot' must be present before the judgment. Without them, no relation can be apprehended.
The passage gives similar examples with rock and snow.
3
Apply to a complex Islamic doctrine
The statement 'the Quran is the uncreated word of Allah' requires concepts of 'Quran', 'word', 'Allah', and 'uncreated'. The assent ties them together.
Disagreements about the concepts can lead to erroneous assents.

الرياضية والاستدلالات العقلية المختلفة، كاستنتاج العقل وجود المؤثر عند ملاحظة الأثر، واستنباط المعاني المجرَّدة من ظواهر الأشياء المدركة بالحس، وتتم الاستنتاجات والاستنباطات العقلية بملاحظة الذهن للمدركات الواردة إليه من الخارج، وذلك بما منحه الله من استعداد فطري للاستنتاج، وللاستنباط، وبما وضع فيه من قدرة يستطيع بها تمييز الحقائق، وتحليل المركبات إلى عناصرها، وتركيب العناصر في صورة منسجمة، واستخلاص المعاني الكلية من الجزئيات المتشابهة، كما وضع في الحواس قدرات تستطيع بها تمييز صفات الأشياء، ولولا هذه القدرات الفطرية لاختلت موازين المدركات سواء أكانت مدركات حِسِّيَّةً أو مدركات عقلية.
وحينما نراقب ما في أذهاننا من مدركات مختلفة واردة إليها من العالم الخارج عنها، أو حاصلة فيها مما استنتجته قدرات الاستنتاج فيها بالنظر فيما ورد إليها من الخارج؛ فإننا نستطيع تقسيمها إلى قسمين رئيسين:
أ ـ فهي إما أن تكون مجرد مفردات منبثة تقع صورت haunt في الذهن، دون أن يحكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، ولنسم هذه (تصورات) فهذا هو القسم الأول وهو (التصورات).
ب ـ وإما أن تكون هذه المفردات الواقعة صورتها في الذهن قد حكم الذهن بإثبات أو نفي علاقات بينها، سواء أكان الذهن مصيباً في حكمه أو مخطئاً، وسواء أوصل إلى درجة الجزم أو لم يصل، ولنسم هذه (تصديقات) وهذا هو القسم الثاني وهو (التصديقات).
تعريف التصور والتصديق:
فالتصور: هو إدراك أي مفرد من مفردات الأشياء والمعاني.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Distinction between Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author explains the difference between conceptual perception and assent with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptual perception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) in any mental act, and identify the prior conceptual perceptions within a given judgment from the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every judgment (tasdiq) contains prior concepts (tasawwur), but not every concept leads to a judgment.

This passage presents the foundational distinction in Islamic logic between tasawwur and tasdiq. Understanding this is crucial because all knowledge in the Islamic sciences—whether fiqh, kalam, or tafsir—begins with concepts that are then combined into judgments. The author explains that we only move from isolated concepts to proper knowledge when we perceive a relation between them, whether positive or negative. This lesson will help you identify these two components in any intellectual act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mental perception
From the passage, locate tasawwur (conceptual perception) and tasdiq (assent). Notice that tasawwur deals with single terms, while tasdiq involves a relation between terms.
2
Find the condition for transition
The author states that perception of a relation (nisba) between concepts marks the move from tasawwur to tasdiq. This relation can be affirmative or negative.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the rock-hardness example (affirmative) and snow-heat example (negative). See how each starts with separate concepts, then a perceived relation yields a judgment.
4
Recognize the inclusion of tasawwur in tasdiq
The passage emphasizes that every tasdiq includes prior tasawwurat. Check the examples: the judgment 'rock is hard' presupposes the concepts 'rock' and 'hardness'.
5
Apply to a new Islamic example
Take the statement 'Allah is powerful' from tafsir. Identify the concepts (Allah, powerful) and the relation (affirmative). This is a tasdiq built on prior tasawwur.

Forms in our minds with certain relations, and these relations (i.e., connections) are either affirmative or negative. When we perceive these relations, we have issued our intellectual judgment.
Here it becomes clear to us that as long as we are within the limits of perceiving scattered single terms, without perceiving relations between them in the mind, we remain within the limits of conceptual perception. However, if we perceive a relation (a connection), then at that point we move from conceptual perception, which was alone, to assent along with it. Every assent is inseparable from prior conceptual perceptions within it that preceded it in the occurrence of perception.
Examples:
A) Among the images of single terms that occur in our minds are the image of rock and the image of hardness. Then we perceive that between rock and hardness there is a relation of a qualified thing to a quality. This relation is the connection we perceived. So when we decide that rock has the quality of hardness, we have issued a positive judgment. This judgment is an assent that came after conceptual perceptions not combined with a judgment.
B) Among the images of single terms that occur in our minds are the image of snow and the image of heat. This perception of ours is still within the limits of conceptual perception. When we perceive that between snow and heat there is a negative relation, we have issued our intellectual judgment that snow is not hot. Our perception of this negative relation is an assent that came after prior conceptual perceptions not combined with a judgment.
Thus we can analogize from these two examples all mental perceptions, both conceptual and assented.
The field of mental percepts:
Food, Stone
Snow, Hard, Beautiful
Hot, Sky, Face, Work
Cold, Fire, Ugly, Good
These are conceptual single terms.
Good food, Snow is not hot
Sky is cold, Face is beautiful
Unpleasant deed, Hard stone
These sentences contain assented relations.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two fundamental types of perception: tasawwur (conceptual perception) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by explaining that we form images in our minds along with certain relations—either affirmative or negative. Perceiving these relations constitutes an intellectual judgment. The key distinction is drawn: as long as we only perceive isolated single terms without any relation between them, we remain in the realm of tasawwur. Once we perceive a relation (positive or negative) between two terms, we move to tasdiq. Importantly, every tasdiq necessarily includes prior tasawwurat within it. Two examples are given: the affirmative relation between rock and hardness yields the judgment “rock is hard”; the negative relation between snow and heat yields “snow is not hot.” Both are tasdiq built on prior tasawwur. Finally, a diagram lists conceptual terms (like food, stone, snow) and then illustrates how they combine into propositions with assented relations (like “good food,” “snow is not hot”). The lesson establishes that the foundation of logic lies in understanding this transition from isolated concepts to judgments.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
نسبة
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must occur for us to move from conceptual perception (tasawwur) to assent (tasdiq)?
A) We must perceive a relation between two concepts.
B) We must memorize all single terms.
C) We must utter a sentence aloud.
D) We must negate a concept.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental distinction in Islamic logic: tasawwur is isolated concepts, tasdiq is concepts combined with a perceived relation forming a judgment.

Defining Perception and Distinguishing Knowledge
Author defines perception and divides it into necessary and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between idrak (perception) and 'ilm (knowledge) according to the author's definitions, and identify whether a given perception is necessary (daruri) based on the author's classification.

Core Idea
Perception is broader than knowledge; knowledge is only veridical perception.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial terminological distinction that underpins Islamic epistemology. He defines perception in the broadest sense—any mental form—and then restricts the term knowledge to perceptions that match reality. This distinction prevents confusion and sets the stage for the division of perception into necessary and acquired types, which is essential for understanding how we come to know truths in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of idrak
Find the Arabic phrase 'حصول صورة ما لأي شيء في الذهن'. This phrase captures the essence: perception is the acquisition of a form of something in the mind. Note that the author explicitly states that certainty and correspondence to reality are not required.
The definition is inclusive: any mental representation, true or false, certain or uncertain.
2
Identify the author's restriction of 'ilm
The author says he prefers to restrict 'ilm to perceptions that conform to reality. Look for the phrase 'نؤثر أن نخص لفظة العلم بما كان مطابقاً للواقع'. This distinguishes his usage from that of logicians who use 'ilm more broadly.
This terminological choice aims to avoid confusion and unify terminology.
3
Recognize the division of perception
The author divides mental perception into two parts. The first part is 'al-idrak al-daruri' (necessary perception). Note the definition: 'الذي لا يحتاج الذهن فيه إلى' (that which the mind does not need...). The text cuts off, but the implication is that necessary perception is self-evident without effort.
The second part (acquired perception) is expected but not present in this passage.
4
Apply the definitions to examples
Given a statement like 'I have the concept of salah in my mind' — this is idrak because it is a mental form, regardless of its truth. But 'I know that salah is obligatory' — if it conforms to reality, it would be 'ilm as per the author.
Practice distinguishing idrak and 'ilm using Islamic concepts.

Perception and its divisions:
Perception is the acquisition of a form of something in the mind, whether it reaches the level of verification and certainty or does not reach that, and whether it is in conformity with reality and truth or not in conformity with that. The logicians may call this knowledge (ilm), but we prefer to restrict the term knowledge to what conforms to reality, as non-logicians do, in order to avoid confusion and unify terminology.

This passage defines perception (idrak) in a broad sense: any mental representation, regardless of certainty or correspondence to reality. The author notes that logicians sometimes call this 'knowledge' (ilm), but the author prefers to restrict knowledge to cognition that matches reality, to avoid ambiguity and standardize terms. Then perception is divided into two types: necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi, which is expected but not stated due to cut-off). Necessary perception is that which the mind does not need (to actively acquire or reflect upon? The text stops). This division is foundational for epistemology and logic, as necessary propositions are self-evident and form the basis of proof.

Defining Perception and Restricting 'Ilm
Author defines idrak and restricts 'ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the author's definition of idrak from the logicians' usage of 'ilm and explain why the author restricts 'ilm.

Core Idea
Perception is any mental image; knowledge is only true and certain perception.

Before diving into divisions, the author clarifies two key terms: idrak (perception) and 'ilm (knowledge). This terminological precision is crucial for understanding later debates in logic and epistemology. The author deliberately narrows 'ilm to match common Islamic usage, avoiding confusion with broader logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of idrak
Find the sentence where the author defines perception. Note that it includes any mental image regardless of certainty or correspondence to reality.
The Arabic begins: الإدراك هو حصول صورة ما لأي شيء في الذهن
2
Identify the author's restriction on 'ilm
The author notes that logicians sometimes call this broad perception 'ilm, but he prefers to restrict 'ilm to perceptions that correspond to reality and are certain.
Key phrase: نؤثر أن نخص لفظة العلم بما كان مطابقاً للواقع
3
Compare with logicians' usage
Understand that the author is consciously diverging from some logicians to unify terminology and avoid confusion. This is a methodological choice.
The author says: بعداً عن الاشتباه وتوحيداً للاصطلاح

الإدراك وأقسامه: الإدراك هو حصول صورة ما لأي شيء في الذهن سواء أبلغ مبلغ التحقق والجزم أو لم يبلغ ذلك، وسواءً أكان مطابقاً للواقع والحقيقة أو غير مطابق لذلك، وقد يسمي المناطقة هذا علماً، ولكن نؤثر أن نخص لفظة العلم بما كان مطابقاً للواقع كما يفعل غير المناطقة، بعداً عن الاشتباه وتوحيداً للاصطلاح. أقسامه: والإدراك الذهني مهما كان شأنه ينقسم إلى قسمين: القسم الأول: الإدراك الضروري، وهو الذي لا يحتاج الذهن فيه إلى طلب بالدليل، وإنما يلتقطه بالبداهة، كإدراكنا وجود أنفسنا، وكإدراكنا صورة الحرارة والبرودة، ومعنى الوجود والعدم، والكبر والصغر، والزمان والمكان، وكإدراكنا أن النار حارة، وأن الصخر جامد، وأن الرياح تحرك أغصان الأشجار، إلى غير ذلك من معارف بدهية لا تحصى . وهذا الإدراك الضروري يتناول قسمي التصور والتصديق . القسم الثاني : الإدراك النظري، أي الإدراك المكتسب بالتأمل والنظر في الأدلة، التي ينتقل الذهن فيها أو بوساطتها من المعلوم إلى المجهول، فهو إذن ما يُحتاج في تحصيله إلى عملية من عمليات الاستدلال الفكري . والنظر: هو ملاحظة المعلومات الموجودة في الذهن للوصول منها إلى مجهولات تصورية أو تصديقية . ومن أمثلة المدركات النظرية ما نستنتجه في الرياضيات بالتأمل والنظر، كجذر عدد من الأعداد جذراً تربيعياً أو تكعيبياً، وكالتوصل من ملاحظة تغير العالم إلى معرفة حدوثه، ومن معرفة حدوثه إلى العلم بأنه بحاجة إلى محدث، وكالتوصل إلى تصور فاكهة مجهولة أو أرض مجهولة أو دار أو آلة عن طريق وصفها، أو عن طريق النظر إلى بعض أجزائها أو آثارها، كتصور أحد علماء الحيوان هيكل حيوان منقرض إذ عثر على بعض عظام من هيكله العظمي، فأخذ يستكمل بقية أجزاء الهيكل من تخيله، ملاحظاً ما تقتضيه القواعد العامة المستفادة من التكوين الحيواني، وما زال يتابع تصوره حتى رسم الحيوان كله، وسخر منه الناس يومئذ، ومات الرجل وبعد مدة عثر الباحثون على هيكل كامل للحيوان الذي تصوره ذلك العالم الحيواني عن طريق التأمل والنظر، وصدق الواقع حدسه . ومن أمثلة المدركات النظرية ما يتوصل إليه الباحث الجنائي إذ يتعرف على المجرم عن طريق الأدلة والأمارات، ومعظم الحقائق العلمية التي توصل إليها العلماء في مختلف العلوم هي من المدركات النظرية، وقد تغدو المدركات النظرية بعد الوصول إليها وتمكنها من النفس مدركات ضرورية .
Perception and its divisions: Perception is the occurrence of an image of anything in the mind, whether it reaches the level of verification and certainty or does not reach that, and whether it corresponds to reality and truth or does not correspond to that. The logicians sometimes call this ‘knowledge’, but we prefer to specify the word ‘knowledge’ for what corresponds to reality, as non-logicians do, to avoid confusion and unify terminology. Its divisions: Mental perception, whatever its status, divides into two divisions: The first division: Necessary perception, which is what the mind does not need to seek through proof, but rather seizes it by intuition, like our perception of our own existence, and our perception of the image of heat and cold, and the meaning of existence and non-existence, and bigness and smallness, and time and place, and our perception that fire is hot, that rock is solid, that winds move the branches of trees, and other such self-evident cognitions that cannot be counted. This necessary perception encompasses both conceptualization and assent. The second division: Theoretical perception, i.e., perception acquired through reflection and examination of proofs, by which or through which the mind moves from the known to the unknown. It is therefore what requires a process of intellectual reasoning. And reflection (nazar) is: the observation of information present in the mind to reach from them unknown conceptual or assenting matters. Examples of theoretical perceptions include what we deduce in mathematics through reflection and examination, like the root of a number, square or cube root, and like arriving from observing the change of the world to knowing its temporal origination, and from knowing its origination to knowing that it needs an originator, and like arriving at the conception of an unknown fruit or unknown land or house or machine through its description, or through examining some of its parts or effects, like the conception of a zoologist of the skeleton of an extinct animal when he found some bones of its skeleton, so he began to complete the rest of the skeleton from his imagination, observing what general rules derived from animal structure require, and he continued his conception until he drew the entire animal, and people mocked him at that time, and the man died, and after a while researchers found a complete skeleton of the animal that that zoologist had conceived through reflection and examination, and reality confirmed his intuition. And examples of theoretical perceptions include what the criminal investigator arrives at when he identifies the criminal through evidence and clues. And most scientific facts that scientists have arrived at in various sciences are from theoretical perceptions. And theoretical perceptions may, after being reached and becoming firmly established in the soul, become necessary perceptions.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines perception (idrak) broadly as any mental image, regardless of certainty or correspondence to reality. The author then notes a terminological preference: logicians often call this 'ilm (knowledge), but the author restricts 'ilm to certain and true perceptions to avoid ambiguity. The main division is into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). Necessary perceptions are self-evident, requiring no reasoning; examples include awareness of one's own existence, heat/cold, spatial/temporal concepts, and immediate truths like 'fire is hot.' The author emphasizes that necessary perception covers both conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Theoretical perceptions are acquired through reasoning (nazar), moving from known premises to unknown conclusions. Nazar itself is defined as examining known information to reach new conceptual or assenting knowledge. Several illustrative examples are given: mathematical derivations, inferring the world's origination from its change (a classic kalam argument), conceptualizing unknown objects via description or traces, and even a story of a zoologist reconstructing an extinct animal's skeleton from partial remains. The passage concludes by noting that theoretical perceptions can become habitual and feel necessary, though they are originally acquired.

Key Terms
إدراك
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because logicians define 'ilm too narrowly
B) To avoid confusion and unify terminology
C) Because only certain perceptions are useful
D) To align with Greek philosophical tradition

Key Takeaway
Perception is any mental image, but 'ilm (knowledge) is reserved for true and certain perceptions.

Necessary vs. Theoretical Perception
Division of perception into necessary and theoretical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given perception as necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari) based on the criterion of requiring proof, using examples from the text.

Core Idea
Necessary perception is self-evident; theoretical perception requires reasoning.

The author divides all mental perception into two fundamental types: necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). Necessary perceptions are immediately grasped without effort, while theoretical perceptions are acquired through reflection (nazar). This division is foundational in Islamic logic and theology for distinguishing between basic axioms and derived knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Find the sentence where the author splits perception into two parts: necessary and theoretical.
The Arabic: الإدراك الذهني مهما كان شأنه ينقسم إلى قسمين
2
Understand the criterion for necessary perception
Necessary perception does not require seeking proof; it is seized by intuition (badaha). The author lists many examples.
Key phrase: لا يحتاج الذهن فيه إلى طلب بالدليل، وإنما يلتقطه بالبداهة
3
Understand the criterion for theoretical perception
Theoretical perception is acquired through reflection (nazar) and reasoning, moving from known to unknown.
Key phrase: الإدراك المكتسب بالتأمل والنظر في الأدلة
4
Test with examples
Use the author's examples to practice classifying: e.g., 'fire is hot' is necessary; 'the world is originated' is theoretical.
The author gives examples for both types.
5
Note that nazar is defined separately
The author defines nazar as the observation of known information to reach unknown matters, which is the process behind theoretical perception.
Definition: ملاحظة المعلومات الموجودة في الذهن للوصول منها إلى مجهولات

The author then notes a terminological preference: logicians often call this 'ilm (knowledge), but the author restricts 'ilm to certain and true perceptions to avoid ambiguity. The main division is into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). Necessary perceptions are self-evident, requiring no reasoning; examples include awareness of one's own existence, heat/cold, spatial/temporal concepts, and immediate truths like 'fire is hot.' The author emphasizes that necessary perception covers both conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Theoretical perceptions are acquired through reasoning (nazar), moving from known premises to unknown conclusions. Nazar itself is defined as examining known information to reach new conceptual or assenting knowledge. Several illustrative examples are given: mathematical derivations, inferring the world's origination from its change (a classic kalam argument), conceptualizing unknown objects via description or traces, and even a story of a zoologist reconstructing an extinct animal's skeleton from partial remains. The passage concludes by noting that theoretical perceptions can become habitual and feel necessary, though they are originally acquired.

Distinguishing Necessary from Theoretical Perception
Classifies perception into necessary and theoretical, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given perception as necessary or theoretical by applying the criteria of self-evidence and inferential need.

Core Idea
Perception is either self-evident or inferred; this distinction underlies all Islamic epistemology.

In Islamic logic and epistemology, knowledge is either self-evident (daruri) or acquired through reflection (nazari). This classification is foundational for building valid arguments and avoiding errors in reasoning. The passage before you defines these two types and provides vivid examples, including the famous Kalam cosmological argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of perception
The passage explicitly names necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) perception. Note that necessary perception includes both concepts and assents.
2
Define the key term 'nazar'
Nazar is defined as observing known information to reach unknown concepts or assents. Understand this as the engine of theoretical perception.
3
Analyse the examples given
For each example (math, cosmology, zoology, criminal investigation), decide whether it is a concept or assent, and why it requires inference.
4
Apply the classification
Given a new example from an Islamic science, determine if it is necessary or theoretical, and justify your answer.
Notice that the passage ends by noting that theoretical perceptions can become necessary once firmly established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الإدراك الضروري يتناول قسمي التصور والتصديق . القسم الثاني : الإدراك النظري، أي الإدراك المكتسب بالتأمل والنظر في الأدلة، التي ينتقل الذهن فيها أو بوساطتها من المعلوم إلى المجهول، فهو إذن ما يُحتاج في تحصيله إلى عملية من عمليات الاستدلال الفكري . والنظر: هو ملاحظة المعلومات الموجودة في الذهن للوصول منها إلى مجهولات تصورية أو تصديقية . ومن أمثلة المدركات النظرية ما نستنتجه في الرياضيات بالتأمل والنظر، كجذر عدد من الأعداد جذراً تربيعياً أو تكعيبياً، وكالتوصل من ملاحظة تغير العالم إلى معرفة حدوثه، ومن معرفة حدوثه إلى العلم بأنه بحاجة إلى محدث، وكالتوصل إلى تصور فاكهة مجهولة أو أرض مجهولة أو دار أو آلة عن طريق وصفها، أو عن طريق النظر إلى بعض أجزائها أو آثارها، كتصور أحد علماء الحيوان هيكل حيوان منقرض إذ عثر على بعض عظام من هيكله العظمي، فأخذ يستكمل بقية أجزاء الهيكل من تخيله، ملاحظاً ما تقتضيه القواعد العامة المستفادة من التكوين الحيواني، وما زال يتابع تصوره حتى رسم الحيوان كله، وسخر منه الناس يومئذ، ومات الرجل وبعد مدة عثر الباحثون على هيكل كامل للحيوان الذي تصوره ذلك العالم الحيواني عن طريق التأمل والنظر، وصدق الواقع حدسه . ومن أمثلة المدركات النظرية ما يتوصل إليه الباحث الجنائي إذ يتعرف على المجرم عن طريق الأدلة والأمارات، ومعظم الحقائق العلمية التي توصل إليها العلماء في مختلف العلوم هي من المدركات النظرية، وقد تغدو المدركات النظرية بعد الوصول إليها وتمكنها من النفس مدركات ضرورية .
This necessary perception includes the two divisions: concept and assent. The second division: theoretical perception, i.e., perception acquired by reflection and examination of proofs, by which or through which the mind moves from the known to the unknown. It is therefore that whose acquisition requires a process of intellectual reasoning. And examination (nazar) is: the observation of information present in the mind to arrive from it to unknown concepts or assents. Examples of theoretical perceptions include what we deduce in mathematics by reflection and examination, such as the square root or cube root of a number, and arriving from observing the change of the world to knowing its temporal origination, and from knowing its temporal origination to knowing that it needs a creator, and arriving at a concept of an unknown fruit, land, house, or machine by describing it, or by examining some of its parts or traces, such as a zoologist's conception of an extinct animal's skeleton when he found some bones from its skeleton, then he completed the rest of the skeleton from his imagination, observing what the general rules derived from animal formation require, and he continued his conception until he drew the entire animal. People mocked him then, and the man died. After a while, researchers found a complete skeleton of the animal that zoologist had conceived by reflection and examination, and reality verified his intuition. Examples of theoretical perceptions also include what a criminal investigator arrives at by identifying the criminal through evidence and clues. Most scientific facts that scientists have discovered in various sciences are theoretical perceptions. Theoretical perceptions may, after being reached and established in the soul, become necessary perceptions.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage classifies perception into two types. First, necessary perception (idrak daruri) is self-evident and requires no intellectual effort. The text mentions examples like awareness of our own existence, heat and cold, existence and non-existence, and basic physical facts. It then states that necessary perception covers both conceptual (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) knowledge. Second, theoretical perception (idrak nazari) is acquired through reflection and examination (nazar) of evidence. Nazar itself is defined as the mental process of moving from known information to unknown concepts or assents. The passage provides several vivid examples: mathematical reasoning (square roots), cosmological argument (change implies temporality, which implies a creator), and conceptual reconstruction (a zoologist inferring an extinct animal's skeleton from partial bones). These examples illustrate how theoretical perceptions are built on evidence and inference. The passage concludes by noting that theoretical perceptions, once firmly established, can become so ingrained that they feel self-evident, blurring the line between necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Rational vs Customary Implication in Logic
Distinguishes rational and customary implication for logicians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rational implication from customary implication and explain why logicians exclude customary implication from logical demonstration.

Core Idea
Reason alone guarantees necessary entailment; custom is not logic.

In this passage, the author clarifies a critical disciplinary boundary: logic deals only with necessary relations that reason can grasp independently of experience. You will learn to identify when implication is rational versus customary, and why customary implication—though legitimate in rhetoric and legal inference—has no place in logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of implication
The author presents two categories: rational (عقلي) implication, which the mind judges by itself, and customary (عرفي) implication, which requires repeated observation of reality. Find the Arabic terms and their definitions.
The passage uses 'لزوم عقلي' and 'لزوم عرفي'.
2
Determine the basis of each type
Rational implication is a necessary connection; the mind cannot conceive of the opposite without contradiction. Customary implication is based on habit or frequent occurrence; the connection is not rationally necessary.
Example: Fire burning is customary, not rational.
3
Apply the logicians' criterion
Logicians (المناطقة) consider only rational concomitant indication as valid for logical inference. Ask yourself: why? Because they seek certainty and universality, which custom cannot provide. Identify where the author states this rule in the Arabic.
The phrase 'المعتبر من الدلالة الالتزامية عند المناطقة هي العقلية فقط' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ قد عرفنا أن اللزوم قد يكون لزوماً عقلي الثبوت، أي يحكم العقل المجرد به، وقد يكون لزوماً عرفياً، أي لا يحكم العقل به إلا بعد ملاحظة الواقع، وتكرر مشاهدة اللزوم فيه، دون أن يكون لدى العقل ما يقتضي هذا اللزوم، إذ قد عرفنا هذا فلا بد من التنبيه على أن المعتبر من الدلالة الالتزامية عند المناطقة هي الدلالة الالتزامية العقلية فقط، أما العرفية فلا اعتبار لها عندهم، إنما لها اعتبار في دلالات الكلام بوجه عام، ويعتمد عليها عند علماء البلاغة، ويؤخذ من طريقها مستنبطات فقهية وغيرها عند الأصوليين، وقد يُحتج بها عندهم لدى المناظرة.
And since we have known that implication may be rational in affirmation, i.e., the bare mind judges it, and it may be customary implication, i.e., the mind does not judge it except after observing reality and repeatedly witnessing the implication in it, without the mind having what necessitates this implication. Since we have known this, it is necessary to point out that what is considered valid from concomitant indication according to the logicians is only rational concomitant indication. As for customary, it has no consideration with them; it only has consideration in the indications of speech in general, and it is relied upon by the scholars of rhetoric, and through its path are taken legal inferences and others among the usuli scholars, and it may be used as evidence in their disputation.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial distinction for the discipline of logic (mantiq). The author reminds that implication (luzūm) can be either rational (ʿaqlī) — where pure reason alone determines the connection — or customary (ʿurfī) — where the connection is learned from repeated observation of reality. Customary implication is not rationally necessary; the mind sees it only after experience. The author then clarifies that logicians (al-munāṭiqa) only consider rational concomitant indication as valid within their science. Customary concomitant indication has no logical weight, but it does have validity in other fields: it is used in rhetoric (balāgha) and in legal inference (istinbāṭ) by usul scholars, and can be used as evidence in disputation (munāẓara). This boundary is important: logic deals with necessary truths, while customary connections belong to empirical or conventional domains.

Key Terms
لزوم عقلي
لزوم عرفي
دلالة التزامية

Evident Implication: Two Senses (Akhass and A'am)
Footnote distinguishing two types of evident implication in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of evident concomitant and identify examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Some implications are obvious from one concept alone; others need comparison.

In the study of rational implication (al-luzūm al-ʿaqlī), logicians refined the category of evident concomitant (lāzim bayyin) into two technical senses. The more specific (akhass) sense requires only the subject to be conceptualized for the concomitant to be grasped, while the more general (a'am) sense calls for both the subject and something else (like a difference from another species) to be considered. This distinction sharpens our understanding of how concepts relate and is crucial for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'lāzim bayyin'
A 'lāzim bayyin' (evident concomitant) is a mental implication that is immediately recognizable when the subject (malzūm) is conceptualized. The passage divides this into two types based on how many concepts are needed to see the implication.
2
Identify the condition for the more specific sense (akhass)
For the akhass, merely conceptualizing the subject suffices to bring the concomitant to mind. Example: conceptualizing the number four immediately implies that it can be split into two equal integers.
3
Identify the condition for the more general sense (a'am)
For the a'am, you need to conceptualize both the subject and something else (e.g., a second concept) to see the implication. Example: to see that 'difference from horse' is a concomitant of 'human', you must think of both 'human' and 'horse'.
4
Observe the inclusion relationship
The more general sense (a'am) includes the more specific (akhass), because the rule for a'am also covers cases where only the subject is needed (since you could still conceptualize the subject alone, but the definition of a'am formally requires both). The akhass is a subset of a'am.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic examples
Practice by identifying which type an implication belongs to in examples from fiqh, kalam, or logic. For instance, from the concept of 'Allah', existence (wujud) is immediately implied (akhass), while attributes like speech may require additional context (a'am).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) يخصّ بعض المناطقة اللزوم العقلي هذا ويسمّي اللزوم الذهني أيضاً باسم «اللازم البيّن بالمعنى الأخص» اصطلاحاً، وضابطه أنّه الذي يلزم من تصوّر ملزومه فقط تصوّره، كتصوّر الانقسام إلى عددين متساويين صحيحين لازماً ذهنياً لتصوّر عدد الأربعة. أمّا ما يلزم من تصوّر اللازم وتصوّر الملزوم معاً تصوّر اللزوم بينهما، فيُطلق عليه اصطلاحاً اسم «اللازم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم»، ومن أمثلته تصوّر الإنسان إذا اقترن بتصوّر مغايرته للفرس مثلاً تصوّر أن هذه المغايرة لازم للإنسان. وأُطلق عليه اصطلاحاً عنوان «اللازم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم» لأنّ ضابطه يشمل البيّن بالمعنى الأخص وغيره، إذ اللازم البيّن بالمعنى الأخص ينطبق عليه هذا الضابط أيضاً.
(1) Some logicians specify this rational implication and also call mental implication by the term 'the evident concomitant in the more specific sense' (al-lāzim al-bayyin bi-l-maʿnā al-akhass) terminologically, and its rule is that its conceptualization results from conceptualizing only its subject (malzūm), like conceptualizing division into two equal integers as a mental concomitant of conceptualizing the number four. As for what results from conceptualizing the concomitant and conceptualizing the subject together, conceptualizing the implication between them, it is terminologically called 'the evident concomitant in the more general sense' (al-lāzim al-bayyin bi-l-maʿnā al-ʿamm). An example is conceptualizing 'human' when paired with conceptualizing its difference from 'horse' for instance, conceptualizing that this difference is a concomitant of 'human'. And it was given the terminological title 'the evident concomitant in the more general sense' because its rule includes the evident in the more specific sense and others, since the evident in the more specific sense also falls under this rule.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This footnote provides a refined classification of rational implication (al-luzūm al-ʿaqlī) which was previously stated to be the only type considered by logicians. Some logicians (baʿḍ al-munāṭiqa) further divide evident implication (al-lāzim al-bayyin) into two categories:

1. Evident in the more specific sense (al-bayyin bi-l-maʿnā al-akhass): Here, merely conceptualizing the subject (malzūm) suffices to conceptualize the concomitant. For example, conceptualizing the number four immediately brings to mind that it can be divided into two equal integers (2+2). The concomitant is directly grasped from the subject alone.

2. Evident in the more general sense (al-bayyin bi-l-maʿnā al-ʿamm): Here, conceptualizing the concomitant requires conceptualizing both the subject and something else (e.g., its difference from another species). For example, to conceive that 'difference from horse' is a concomitant of 'human', you need to conceptualize both 'human' and 'horse' and see the relation. The label 'general' applies because this rule covers both the first type (where difference is already implied) and more complex cases. The distinction clarifies that some implications are immediately obvious from one concept, while others require comparison.

The author adds that the first type (akhass) is included under the second (a'am) because the rule of the broader sense (requiring both concepts) also applies if we consider the subject's concept alone, but the narrower sense adds specificity.

Key Terms
لازم بيّن بالمعنى الأخص
لازم بيّن بالمعنى الأعم

Intension and Extension (Concept and Referent)
Author distinguishes between the mental concept and the actual referents of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the intension (mafhum) and extension (musadaq) of a term in definitions.

Core Idea
Intension is the mental concept; extension is the actual individuals.

When we use a word, we must separate what it means in the mind from what it refers to in reality. This lesson clarifies that distinction using examples from Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the terms 'mafhum' and 'musadaq' in the Arabic passage. Note that they are the two central technical terms.
2
Match each with its definition
Understand that 'mafhum' is the mental meaning evoked by the word, while 'musadaq' is the actual individual(s) to which the word applies.
3
Apply to a particular word
Take the example 'Makkah'. Its intension is the mental image and attributes; its extension is the actual city of Makkah itself.
4
Apply to a universal word
Take 'city'. Its intension is the essential attributes (buildings, streets, etc.); its extension is every actual place with those attributes.
The universal disregards non-essential features.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفهوم والماصدق
لدى أهل هذا الفن اصطلاحان هما المفهوم والماصدق.
أما المفهوم: فيقصدون به المعنى الذهني الذي يثيره اللفظ في الأذهان، واللفظ دلالة كلامية عليه.
وأما الماصدق: فيقصدون به الفرد أو الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها اللفظ إذ يتحقق فيها مفهومه الذهني.
فاللفظ الجزئي يثير في الذهن الصورة والصفات التي نعرفها عما يدل عليه هذا اللفظ، مثل: (مكة - نهر الفرات - عمر بن الخطاب) هذا هو مفهوم اللفظ، أما ماصدقه فهو مكة البلد الحرام نفسه، ونهر الفرات ذاته، وعمر بن الخطاب عينه.
واللفظ الكلي يثير في الذهن الصفات الأساسية الجوهرية التي يشترك فيها أفراد هذا الكلي، أي جميع ماصدقاته، دون الصفات غير الأساسية وغير الجوهرية التي قد توجد في بعض الأفراد فقط، دون أن تختل دلالة اللفظ، ودون أن يختل انطباق المفهوم الذهني، مثل كلمة: (مدينة) فهذا اللفظ كلي يثير في الذهن فكرة عامة عن مساحة كبيرة من الأرض، فيها أبنية وشوارع وسكان ونشاط اجتماعي وما إلى ذلك مما هو متوافر في كل مدينة، مع صرف النظر عن الفروق أو المزايا الخاصة بكل مدينة منها، ولذلك فهو ينطبق على كل بلد فيه هذه الصفات العامة، والبلاد التي فيها هذه الصفات هي
Intension and Extension. Among the practitioners of this art there are two technical terms: intension (al-mafhum) and extension (al-musadaq). As for intension: they mean by it the mental meaning that the word evokes in minds, and the word is a verbal indication of it. As for extension: they mean by it the individual or individuals to which the word applies when its mental concept is realized in them. So the particular word evokes in the mind the image and attributes that we know about what the word indicates, such as: (Makkah, Euphrates River, Umar ibn al-Khattab) — this is the intension of the word; as for its extension, it is the sacred city of Makkah itself, the Euphrates River itself, and Umar ibn al-Khattab himself. And the universal word evokes in the mind the essential, substantial attributes that the individuals of this universal share, i.e., all its extensions, without the non-essential, non-substantial attributes that may exist in some individuals only, without affecting the meaning of the word or the applicability of the mental concept, such as the word 'city': this universal word evokes in the mind a general idea of a large expanse of land containing buildings, streets, inhabitants, social activity, and so forth — things found in every city, disregarding the differences or special features of each city. Therefore it applies to every place that has these general attributes, and the places that have these attributes are...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage introduces two fundamental concepts in Islamic logic: al-mafhum (intension/concept) and al-musadaq (extension/referent). The author states that the practitioners of this science use these two terms. Al-mafhum refers to the mental meaning that the word evokes in the mind — the word is a verbal indicator of that meaning. Al-musadaq refers to the actual individual(s) to which the word applies, where its mental concept is realized. The author then illustrates with particular words: e.g., 'Makkah' evokes in the mind the image and known attributes (its concept), but its extension is the actual city of Makkah itself. Similarly for 'Euphrates River' and 'Umar ibn al-Khattab'. Next, the author examines universal words: a universal term like 'city' evokes in the mind the essential, substantial attributes shared by all individuals (e.g., having buildings, streets, population, social activity), disregarding non-essential differences; its extension is every place that possesses these general attributes. The passage underscores the distinction between the mental concept — which is abstract and common to many — and the actual individuals in external reality.

Key Terms
المفهوم
الماصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The distinction between mental concept (mafhum) and actual referent (musadaq) is foundational for precise logical communication in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Meaning and Derivation of al-Maṣadaq (Referent)
Author defines maṣadaq and explains its derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the referents (maṣadaqāt) of a universal term and explain how the term 'maṣadaq' is derived.

Core Idea
Maṣadaq refers to the external individuals, not the mental concept.

The term 'maṣadaq' is essential in logic for distinguishing between a universal concept and its instances. In this lesson, we examine how the author defines it using the example of 'insān' and traces its derivation from the verb 'ṣadaqa'. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to map words to their real-world referents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal term used as an example.
The author uses 'insān' (human). Notice that it calls to mind the general concept of rational animal. What are the essential attributes mentioned? Animality and rationality.
2
Distinguish between essence and accident.
The author says attributes that distinguish individuals (accidents) are not considered in the universal's signification. Only the shared essence matters for the universal's application.
3
Define maṣadaq in your own words.
Using the example, the individuals (humans) are the maṣadaqāt of 'insān'. So maṣadaq is the external individuals to which the term applies.
4
Trace the derivation of the term 'maṣadaq'.
It comes from 'mā' (interrogative/relative) and 'ṣadaqa' (to verify). Originally, one asked 'Upon what does this word verify?' and answered 'It verifies upon such and such.' Hence, they coined 'maṣadaq' for the referents.
5
Apply the concept to another Islamic term.
The author mentions 'zakat' — each specific payment is a maṣadaq of 'zakat'. Try to think of another fiqh term, e.g., 'ṣalāh' — what are its maṣadaqāt? (Each individual prayer.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماصدقات هذا اللفظ. ومثل كلمة: (إنسان) فهذا أيضاً لفظ كلي يثير في الذهن فكرة عامة عن الحيوان الناطق، باعتبار هاتين الصفتين هما الحقيقتان المكونتان لماهية الإنسان، والمشتركتان في كل فردٍ من أفراد الإنسان، أما الصفات التي تميز الأفراد بعضهم عن بعض فلا ينظر إليها في دلالة الكلي، ولذلك فهو ينطبق على كل فرد توجد فيه الحيوانية والناطقية، مهما كانت صفاته الأخرى، وأفراد الناس هم ماصدقات لفظ الإنسان، إذ لفظ إنسان يصدق على كل فرد من أفراد هذا النوع. ولفظ (الماصدق) اسم صناعي مأخوذ في الأصل من كلمة (ما) الاستفهامية أو الموصولية، وكلمة (صدق) التي هي فعل ماضٍ من الصدق، إذ كان يقال مثلاً: على ماذا صدق هذا اللفظ؟ فيقال في الجواب: صَدَق على كذا أو كذا، فاشتقوا من ذلك أو نحتوا كلمة (ماصدق) وعرفوها بأل التعريف فصاروا يقولون: (الماصدق) ويقصدون به ما أوضحنا.
The referents of this word. And like the word 'human', this is also a universal word that arouses in the mind a general idea of the rational animal, considering that these two attributes are the two realities constituting the essence of humanity, and are shared by every individual of humans. As for the attributes that distinguish individuals from one another, they are not considered in the signification of the universal. Therefore, it applies to every individual in which animality and rationality exist, regardless of its other attributes. And humans are the referents of the word 'human', for the word 'human' is true of every individual of this species. And the word 'al-maṣadaq' is an artificial noun originally derived from the interrogative or relative word 'mā' and the word 'ṣadaqa' which is the past tense of truthfulness. For it was said, for example: 'Upon what did this word verify?' and the answer would be: 'It verified upon such and such.' So they derived or coined the word 'maṣadaq' and defined it with the definite article, so they would say 'al-maṣadaq' and mean by it what we have clarified.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'maṣadaq' (referent) which is central to logic. The author begins by illustrating with the universal word 'insān' (human), which calls to mind the general concept of a rational animal. The attributes of animality and rationality constitute the essence (māhiyya) of humanity and are shared by all individuals. Accidental differences like height or color are irrelevant to the universal's meaning. Thus the universal applies to every individual possessing those essential attributes. The author then derives the term 'maṣadaq' from the interrogative 'mā' and the verb 'ṣadaqa' (to be true). Originally, one would ask 'Upon what does this word verify?' and answer 'It verifies upon such and such.' From this, they coined 'maṣadaq' to denote the individuals of which a term is true. For example, the word 'zakat' (obligatory charity) is true of each specific payment, so those payments are its maṣadaqāt. The passage emphasizes that the referent is the external individual, not the mental concept.

Key Terms
الماصدق (al-maṣadaq)
كلي (kullī)

Key Takeaway
The maṣadaq of a universal term is the set of external individuals to which it applies, not the mental concept.
