Understanding Tabāyun (Disparity) Between Terms
Author defines tabāyun as complete difference in concept and referent, with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of tabāyun between two terms in Islamic logical texts and distinguish it from relations like inclusion or overlap.

Core Idea
Tabāyun is complete separation in both meaning and referent—like opposite rulings in fiqh.

In classical Islamic logic, understanding how concepts relate to each other is essential for precise reasoning. The first relation introduced is tabāyun (disparity), which describes two terms that share nothing in common neither in their conceptual content nor in the individuals they refer to. This lesson unpacks this foundational relation using clear examples from everyday language and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of tabāyun
Read the author's definition: tabāyun is the relation between two meanings that are completely different in concept (māfhūm) and whose referents (maṣādiq) do not overlap at all. This means neither term can be truthfully applied to any instance of the other.
In the Arabic, note 'مختلفان مفهوماً مختلفان ما صدقاً' – they differ in both.
2
Check both concept and referent
For two terms to be disparate, they must be distinct in both aspects. For example, 'human' and 'horse' have different concepts (animal species vs. another species) and no individual can be both. If they shared even one individual, they would not be disparate.
This two-part test (concept + referent) is the key logical criterion.
3
Apply the test to the given examples
Examine the three examples: (a) human & horse, (b) male & female, (c) proper names Khālid & Anas. For each, confirm that the concepts are distinct and no individual falls under both. The author explicitly states they are 'completely different' in both.
Proper names differ in concept because each signifies a specific person, not a shared essence.
4
Distinguish tabāyun from other relations
Tabāyun is the strongest form of difference. It is not the same as inclusion (e.g., human & animal) where one concept falls under the other, or overlap (e.g., 'doctor' and 'male') where some individuals share both. In Islamic law, wājib and ḥarām are disparate because no act can be both for the same person simultaneously.
Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analysing legal or theological definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸)
النِّسَبُ فِي دَائِرَةِ المَعَانِي وَالأَلْفَاظِ
لدى إحصاء النِّسب في دائرة الألفاظ والمعاني تنكشف لنا النسب التاليات:
النسبة الأولى : (التباين) وهي النسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مخالف له في المفهوم، ولا ينطبق أيُّ واحدٍ منهما على أي فرد مما ينطبق عليه الآخر، فهما بحسب تعبير أهل هذا الفن مختلفان مفهوماً مختلفان ما صدقاً .
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل كلمتي : (إنسان) و (فرس) فمعنى كل من هاتين الكلمتين مخالف تماماً لمعنى الكلمة الأخرى، ثم إن كل معنى منهما لا ينطبق على أي فرد من الأفراد التي ينطبق عليه الآخر، فما من فرد من أفراد الناس يصح أن يقال عنه (فرس) على وجه الحقيقة، وما من فرد من أفراد الأفراس يصح أن يقال عنه (إنسان) على وجه الحقيقة، إذن فهما مختلفان في المفهوم مختلفان في الماصدق اختلافاً كلياً، فما بينهما من نسبة هي التباين، وهما متباينان بهذا الاعتبار.
ب - ومثل كلمتي : (ذكر) و (أنثى) فهما مختلفان أيضاً اختلافاً كلياً في المفهوم وفي الماصدق، فهما متباينان .
جـ - ومثل كلمتي : (خالد) عَلَماً على شخص من الناس، و(أنس) عَلَماً على شخص آخر، فهما مختلفان في المفهوم وفي الماصدق، فهما متباينان .
(8) The Relations in the Sphere of Meanings and Words. Upon enumerating the relations in the sphere of words and meanings, the following relations are disclosed to us: The first relation: (al-tabāyun) which is the relation between a meaning and another meaning that is contrary to it in concept, and neither of them applies to any individual to which the other applies. So they are, according to the expression of the people of this art, different in concept and different in referent. Examples: A - Like the two words 'human' and 'horse': the meaning of each of these two words is completely contrary to the meaning of the other word, and each meaning does not apply to any individual to which the other applies. For no individual of humans can truthfully be called a horse, and no individual of horses can truthfully be called a human. Thus they are completely different in concept and different in referent, so the relation between them is disparity, and they are disparate in this regard. B - And like the two words 'male' and 'female': they are also completely different in concept and in referent, so they are disparate. C - And like the two words 'Khālid' as a proper name for a person, and 'Anas' as a proper name for another person: they are different in concept and in referent, so they are disparate.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces the first of several relations between terms: tabāyun (disparity). The author defines tabāyun as the relation between two meanings that are completely different in concept (māfhūm) and also have no common external referent (maṣadaq). Using the technical language of logic, they are different in both concept and referent. Three examples illustrate this: First, 'human' and 'horse' – their meanings are entirely distinct, and no individual human can be called a horse (in literal truth), nor vice versa. Thus they are disparate. Second, 'male' and 'female' – these are also completely different in meaning and no individual is both, so they are disparate. Third, proper names 'Khālid' and 'Anas' – even though both refer to humans, the names themselves have distinct meanings (as proper names signify specific individuals) and their referents are different persons, so they are disparate. The passage emphasizes that disparity requires difference in both concept and referent. For instance, in fiqh, the terms 'wājib' (obligatory) and 'ḥarām' (forbidden) are disparate: their meanings are opposite, and no act can be both obligatory and forbidden for the same person at the same time. This relation is foundational for defining concepts and avoiding confusion between terms.

المفهوم

C) 'Red' and 'color' (red is a type of color)
D) 'Book' and 'Quran' (the Quran is a book)

Key Takeaway
Tabāyun is a logical relation requiring complete difference in both concept and referent, foundational for avoiding term confusion.

Tawatu' and Tashkik: Uniformity and Gradation
Defines tawatu' and tashkik relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tawatu' (uniformity) and tashkik (gradation) in universal meanings and give Islamic examples of each.

Core Idea
Tawatu' and tashkik describe how a universal applies to its individuals—either equally or with gradation.

In this lesson, we examine the first two relations from the list: tawatu' (uniformity) and tashkik (gradation). These two relations describe how a universal concept exists in its individual instances. Understanding them is crucial for grasping how terms like 'human' apply equally, while terms like 'light' or 'existence' apply with varying intensity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
For tawatu', the condition is that the universal exists in all its individuals equally, without variation. For tashkik, the condition is that it exists with variation in strength and weakness.
2
Recognise the difference
Tawatu' implies no priority or excess among individuals; tashkik implies gradation. Example: 'human' is tawatu' (all humans equally human), while 'light' is tashkik (sunlight is stronger than moonlight).
3
Apply to Islamic sciences
In Kalam, existence (wujud) is tashkik: Allah's existence is necessary, creation's is contingent. In fiqh, 'forbidden' (haram) is tawatu' (all forbidden acts are equally forbidden).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥ - التواطؤ: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده بالتساوي دون تفاوت.
٦ - التشكيك: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده مع التفاوت قوّةً وضعفاً.
٧ - الترادف: هي نسبة لفظ إلى لفظ من جهة دلالة كلّ منهما على المعنى نفسه الذي يدلّ عليه الآخر.
٨ - الاشتراك: هي نسبة معنى إلى معنى آخر من جهة اشتراكهما في لفظ واحدٍ يدلّ على كلّ منهما، عكس الترادف.
٩ - التخالف: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما، وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان.
١٠ - التضادّ: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً.
١١ - التناقض: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما معاً وعدم إمكان ارتفاعهما معاً، في شيء واحد وزمان واحد.
١٢ - التماثل: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر مساوٍ له في النتيجة.
١٣ - الكلية والجزئية: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كليّاً، والآخر جزئياً من جزئياته.
١٤ - الكلّ والجزء: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه.
١٥ - الإضافة: هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كلّ منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر.
5 - Tawatu' (uniformity): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals equally without variation. 6 - Tashkik (gradation): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals with variation in strength and weakness. 7 - Taraduf (synonymy): It is the relation of a word to a word in terms of each of them indicating the same meaning that the other indicates. 8 - Ishtirak (homonymy): It is the relation of a meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing a single word that indicates each of them, the opposite of synonymy. 9 - Takhaluf (divergence): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their combining, and the possibility of both being absent while place and time are united. 10 - Tadadd (contrariness): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining while place and time are united, but both can be absent together. 11 - Tanaqud (contradiction): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining together and the impossibility of both being absent together, in one thing and one time. 12 - Tamathul (similarity): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in the result. 13 - Universality and particularity: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being universal and the other a particular of its particulars. 14 - Whole and part: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being a whole and the other a part of its parts. 15 - Idafa (relation/correlation): It is the relation between two meanings where the comprehension of each is linked to the comprehension of the other.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of eleven types of logical relations that hold between concepts (meanings) or between words and meanings. The author presents them as a numbered list, each defined concisely. In logical analysis, these relations are essential for understanding how terms connect and for constructing valid arguments. For instance, tawatu' describes a universal that applies equally to all its individuals, like 'human' applied to every human being. Tashkik involves gradation, like 'light applied to the sun and the moon with different intensity. Taraduf and ishtirak are about synonymy and homonymy respectively. Takhaluf and tadadd deal with compatibility and incompatibility of concepts in the same subject at the same time—takhaluf allows both absence and presence, while tadadd allows only absence of both. Tanaqud (contradiction) is the strictest: two contradictory propositions cannot both be true nor both false at the same time and in the same respect. Tamathul is equality in result, while kulliyya/juz'iyya relates universal and particular, and kull/juz' relates whole and part. Idafa involves correlative terms where each is understood through the other, like 'father' and 'son'.

Key Terms
التواطؤ
التشكيك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Tawatu'
B) Tashkik
C) Taraduf
D) Ishtirak

Key Takeaway
Tawatu' and tashkik are two ways a universal applies to its instances: equally or with gradation.

Taraduf and Ishtirak: Synonymy and Homonymy
Defines taraduf and ishtirak relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between taraduf (synonymy) and ishtirak (homonymy) and give Islamic examples of each.

Core Idea
Taraduf is one word for one meaning, ishtirak is one word for two meanings.

This lesson covers the third and fourth relations: taraduf (synonymy) and ishtirak (homonymy). These deal with the relationship between words and meanings. Synonymy occurs when different words indicate the same meaning, while homonymy occurs when a single word indicates multiple distinct meanings. Mastery of these concepts is essential for accurate interpretation of Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Define each relation
Taraduf: relation between two words that indicate the same meaning. Ishtirak: relation between two meanings that share a single word.
They are opposites.
2
Identify examples
Taraduf: Arabic words like 'asad' and 'ghadanfar' (both meaning 'lion'). Ishtirak: the word 'ayn' meaning eye, spring, or spy.
In Islamic texts, 'yad' (hand) can mean power, limb, or favor.
3
Apply to Islamic sciences
In tafsir, ishtirak can cause ambiguity; the mujtahid must determine which meaning is intended. In fiqh, synonyms may affect rulings if specific words carry legal weight.

١٤ - الكلّ والجزء: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة كون أحدهما كلاً والآخر جزءاً من أجزائه.
١٥ - الإضافة: هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كلّ منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر.
5 - Tawatu' (uniformity): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals equally without variation. 6 - Tashkik (gradation): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals with variation in strength and weakness. 7 - Taraduf (synonymy): It is the relation of a word to a word in terms of each of them indicating the same meaning that the other indicates. 8 - Ishtirak (homonymy): It is the relation of a meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing a single word that indicates each of them, the opposite of synonymy. 9 - Takhaluf (divergence): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their combining, and the possibility of both being absent while place and time are united. 10 - Tadadd (contrariness): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining while place and time are united, but both can be absent together. 11 - Tanaqud (contradiction): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining together and the impossibility of both being absent together, in one thing and one time. 12 - Tamathul (similarity): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in the result. 13 - Universality and particularity: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being universal and the other a particular of its particulars. 14 - Whole and part: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being a whole and the other a part of its parts. 15 - Idafa (relation/correlation): It is the relation between two meanings where the comprehension of each is linked to the comprehension of the other.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of eleven types of logical relations that hold between concepts (meanings) or between words and meanings. The author presents them as a numbered list, each defined concisely. In logical analysis, these relations are essential for understanding how terms connect and for constructing valid arguments. For instance, tawatu' describes a universal that applies equally to all its individuals, like 'human' applied to every human being. Tashkik involves gradation, like 'light applied to the sun and the moon with different intensity. Taraduf and ishtirak are about synonymy and homonymy respectively. Takhaluf and tadadd deal with compatibility and incompatibility of concepts in the same subject at the same time—takhaluf allows both absence and presence, while tadadd allows only absence of both. Tanaqud (contradiction) is the strictest: two contradictory propositions cannot both be true nor both false at the same time and in the same respect. Tamathul is equality in result, while kulliyya/juz'iyya relates universal and particular, and kull/juz' relates whole and part. Idafa involves correlative terms where each is understood through the other, like 'father' and 'son'.

Taraduf (synonymy) relates words to a single meaning; ishtirak (homonymy) relates multiple meanings to a single word.

Takhaluf, Tadadd, and Tanaqud: Compatibility and Contradiction
Defines takhaluf, tadadd, and tanaqud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between takhaluf (compatibility), tadadd (contrariness), and tanaqud (contradiction) and apply them to Islamic propositions.

Core Idea
Takhaluf allows both, tadadd allows neither, tanaqud forces one true and one false.

This lesson examines three relations that deal with the possibility of two meanings coexisting or being absent together: takhaluf (compatibility), tadadd (contrariness), and tanaqud (contradiction). These are fundamental in logic for evaluating statements. In Islamic theology, they are used to analyse doctrinal statements, such as the attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Analyse the definitions
Takhaluf: both meanings can be present simultaneously and both can be absent. Tadadd: cannot be present together but can be absent together. Tanaqud: cannot be present together and cannot be absent together.
Tanaqud is the strictest.
2
Identify the key difference
The difference lies in the possibility of absence: in tadadd, both can be absent; in tanaqud, they cannot.
Think of 'black' and 'white' (tadadd: not both present, but both absent if grey) vs. 'human' and 'non-human' (tanaqud: cannot both be present or both absent for the same thing).
3
Apply to Islamic examples
In Kalam, the attributes 'power' and 'knowledge' are takhaluf (they coexist in Allah). 'Movement' and 'rest' in a single body are tadadd. 'Eternal' and 'temporal' applied to the same thing are tanaqud.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

٦ - التشكيك: هي نسبة وجود معنى كليّ في أفراده مع التفاوت قوّةً وضعفاً.
٧ - الترادف: هي نسبة لفظ إلى لفظ من جهة دلالة كلّ منهما على المعنى نفسه الذي يدلّ عليه الآخر.
٨ - الاشتراك: هي نسبة معنى إلى معنى آخر من جهة اشتراكهما في لفظ واحدٍ يدلّ على كلّ منهما، عكس الترادف.
٩ - التخالف: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما، وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان.
١٠ - التضادّ: هي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة عدم إمكان اجتماعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان ولكن يمكن ارتفاعهما معاً.

١٥ - الإضافة: هي نسبة بين معنيين إدراك كلّ منهما مرتبط بإدراك الآخر.
5 - Tawatu' (uniformity): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals equally without variation. 6 - Tashkik (gradation): It is the relation of the existence of a universal meaning in its individuals with variation in strength and weakness. 7 - Taraduf (synonymy): It is the relation of a word to a word in terms of each of them indicating the same meaning that the other indicates. 8 - Ishtirak (homonymy): It is the relation of a meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing a single word that indicates each of them, the opposite of synonymy. 9 - Takhaluf (divergence): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their combining, and the possibility of both being absent while place and time are united. 10 - Tadadd (contrariness): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining while place and time are united, but both can be absent together. 11 - Tanaqud (contradiction): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of the impossibility of their combining together and the impossibility of both being absent together, in one thing and one time. 12 - Tamathul (similarity): It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning equal to it in the result. 13 - Universality and particularity: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being universal and the other a particular of its particulars. 14 - Whole and part: It is the relation between a meaning and another meaning in terms of one being a whole and the other a part of its parts. 15 - Idafa (relation/correlation): It is the relation between two meanings where the comprehension of each is linked to the comprehension of the other.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of eleven types of logical relations that hold between concepts (meanings) or between words and meanings. The author presents them as a numbered list, each defined concisely. In logical analysis, these relations are essential for understanding how terms connect and for constructing valid arguments. For instance, tawatu' describes a universal that applies equally to all its individuals, like 'human' applied to every human being. Tashkik involves gradation, like 'light applied to the sun and the moon with different intensity. Taraduf and ishtirak are about synonymy and homonymy respectively. Takhaluf and tadadd deal with compatibility and incompatibility of concepts in the same subject at the same time—takhaluf allows both absence and presence, while tadadd allows only absence of both. Tamathul is equality in result, while kulliyya/juz'iyya relates universal and particular, and kull/juz' relates whole and part. Tamathul, Kulliyya/Juz'iyya, Kull/Juz', and Idafa: Other Key Relations
Defines tamathul, kulliyya/juz'iyya, kull/juz', and idafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish tamathul, kulliyya/juz'iyya, kull/juz', and idafa, and give Islamic examples of each.

Core Idea
These four relations cover equality, universal-particular, whole-part, and correlation.

This final lesson covers the remaining four relations from the list: tamathul (similarity/equality), kulliyya/juz'iyya (universality and particularity), kull/juz' (whole and part), and idafa (correlation). Each describes a different way concepts relate to each other, from equality to interdependence. These are essential for accurate argumentation in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Define each relation
Tamathul: two meanings are equal in result (e.g., synonyms in a specific effect). Kulliyya/juz'iyya: one meaning is universal and the other is one of its particulars. Kull/juz': one is a whole and the other a part of that whole. Idafa: each meaning is understood only through the other (e.g., father-son).
2
Identify differences
Tamathul is about equality in effect; kulliyya/juz'iyya is about logical inclusion (species-individual); kull/juz' is about part-whole composition (physical or conceptual); idafa is about mutual dependency.
3
Apply Islamic examples
Tamathul: the terms 'wajib' and 'fard' are often used synonymously in fiqh (equal in result). Kulliyya/juz'iyya: 'human' (universal) and 'Zayd' (particular). Kull/juz': 'the Quran' (whole) and 'Surah al-Fatiha' (part). Idafa: 'father' and 'son'.

Direct Perception of Implication: The Mental Process
Defines the mental process of directly perceiving conditional connections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish direct perception of implication from syllogistic inference by identifying the absence of a middle term.

Core Idea
Implication is perceived directly, not deduced through a middle term.

In this passage, the author identifies a key epistemological distinction: some conditional connections are grasped immediately, without reasoning. This lesson unpacks that process and its contrast with syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
What does the author mean by 'direct perception' (إدراك مباشر)? Look for the description of noticing conjunctions between propositions without mediation.
Direct perception is contrasted with syllogism later.
2
Contrast with syllogism
Why does the author emphasize that this process is not like syllogism? Identify the key difference: syllogism produces a new proposition via a middle term; direct perception does not.
Syllogism is indirect inference.
3
Recognize the types of conjunction
List the kinds of conjunction mentioned: inseparable in existence, non-existence, both, contradictory, etc. How does this variety relate to conditional propositions?
These are the raw materials for conditionals.
4
Form the conditional judgment
After perceiving the conjunction, the mind links the two propositions with a conditional connective (رباط شرطي). Why is this a single mental act?
The two propositions become one conditional proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللزوم في القضايا الشرطية
من أنواع الإدراك المباشر عملية عقلية يدرك بها الناظر اللزوم في القضايا.
إن الباحث الناظر في الواقع المدرك بالحس، أو المدرك بالنظر العقلي المجرد، ليلاحظ أن قضية مثلاً مقترنة بأخرى اقتراناً غير منفك، في الوجود، أو في العدم، أو في كليهما، أو مقترن وجود إحداهما بانعدام الأخرى، أو مقترن انعدام إحداهما بوجود الأخرى، أو اقترانهما اقتران تناقض فأيهما وجد انعدم الآخر وأيهما انعدم وجد الآخر.
فيربط بين القضيتين برباط شرطي يدلّ به على ما أدركه عن الواقع لدى نظره الفكري في القضيتين، فالقضيتان معاً قد كانتا مادة الإدراك المباشر، وعندئذٍ يصدر حكماً شرطياً بما أدركه من اقتران، ويعقد بين القضيتين، ويجعل منهما قضية شرطية واحدة.
ولا تشبه هذه العملية العقلية ما يجري في القياس، لأن القياس يقدم قضية جديدة عن طريق ملاحظة شمول حكم قضية لقضية أخرى لا يوجد فيها هذا الحكم، فالقياس استدلال غير مباشر، أما الحكم باللزوم أو عدمه في القضايا فطريقه الإدراك المباشر، ما لم يستخدم لذلك القياس أو دليل آخر من أدلة الاستدلال.
إذن فمن الإدراك المباشر هذه العملية العقلية التي يدرك بها الناظر الباحث واقع اقتران بين قضيتين، فيصدر بالاستناد إليها حكماً شرطياً، فيقول مثلاً :
أ ـ إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة .
ب ـ لو تعدّدت الآلهة تعدّداً بحق لفسد نظام الكون .
جـ ـ إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً .
د ـ الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً .
هـ ـ الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل أو حرف .
و ـ كلما كان اللون أبيض فهو لا أسود .
إلى غير ذلك من أمثلة شرطية كثيرة .
فلنحلل العملية العقلية في المثال الأول :
كلنا ندرك وجود النهار وامتداد بياضه وسلامة الرؤية فيه في أغلب الأحيان، وندرك أن وجوده مقارن لطلوع الشمس وامتداد أشعتها باتجاه مكان النهار في الأرض، ثم ندرك ذهاب النهار وقدوم الليل وإقبال سواده وظلمته، وندرك أن ذهاب النهار مقارن لغروب الشمس وانحجاب أشعتها عن الأرض .
بعد هذه الإدراكات نعقد مباشرة بين قضية وجود النهار وطلوع الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً، وبين قضية وجود الليل وغروب الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً .
ونعلق كل قضية بينها وبين صاحبتها تلازم برباط شرطي، فنقول :
إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالنهار موجود .
إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة في الأفق .
إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالنهار غير موجود .
إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالليل موجود .
إذا كان الليل موجوداً فالشمس غاربة .
إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالليل غير موجود .
كل هذه القضايا أدركناها بشكل مباشر من إدراك التلازم في الواقع بين طلوع الشمس والنهار، وغروبها والليل .
Implication in conditional propositions. It is a type of direct perception: a mental process by which the observer perceives implication in propositions. Indeed, the observer investigating reality perceived by sense, or perceived by pure intellectual contemplation, notices that a proposition, for example, is conjoined with another in an inseparable conjunction, in existence, or in non-existence, or in both, or the existence of one is conjoined with the non-existence of the other, or the non-existence of one is conjoined with the existence of the other, or their conjunction is a contradictory conjunction such that whichever exists, the other ceases to exist, and whichever ceases to exist, the other exists. So he links the two propositions with a conditional connective by which he indicates what he has perceived about reality through his intellectual consideration of the two propositions. The two propositions together were the material of direct perception, and then he issues a conditional judgment based on what he perceived of conjunction, and he ties the two propositions together and makes them one conditional proposition. This mental process does not resemble what occurs in syllogism, because syllogism produces a new proposition by noting the inclusion of the judgment of one proposition in another proposition in which that judgment does not exist. So syllogism is indirect inference. As for the judgment of implication or its absence in propositions, its path is direct perception, unless syllogism or another evidence from the evidences of inference is used for that. So it is from direct perception this mental process by which the investigating observer perceives the reality of conjunction between two propositions, and then based on it he issues a conditional judgment. He says, for example: A. If daytime exists, then the sun is rising. B. If the gods were truly multiple, the system of the universe would be corrupted. C. Either the number is even or odd. D. A thing is either existent or non-existent. E. A word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle. F. Whenever a color is white, it is not black. And so on, many conditional examples. So let us analyze the mental process in the first example: All of us perceive the existence of daytime, the extension of its brightness, and the clarity of vision in it most of the time, and we perceive that its existence is concomitant with the rising of the sun and the extension of its rays towards the place of daytime on earth. Then we perceive the departure of daytime, the coming of night, the approach of its darkness and gloom, and we perceive that the departure of daytime is concomitant with the setting of the sun and the veiling of its rays from the earth. After these perceptions, we directly establish between the proposition of the existence of daytime and the rising of the sun a connection of concomitance, and between the proposition of the existence of night and the setting of the sun a connection of concomitance. And we attach each proposition to its companion with a conditional connective, saying: If the sun is rising on the horizon, then daytime exists. If daytime exists, then the sun is rising on the horizon. If the sun is setting, then daytime does not exist. If the sun is setting, then night exists. If night exists, then the sun is setting. If the sun is rising on the horizon, then night does not exist. All these propositions we perceived directly from the perception of the real concomitance between the rising of the sun and daytime, and between its setting and night.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the mental process of perceiving implication in conditional propositions, classifying it as a type of direct perception (إدراك مباشر). The author begins by describing how an observer, whether through sensory or intellectual observation, notices various kinds of conjunctions between two propositions: inseparable coexistence in existence or non-existence, contradictory conjunction, etc. The observer then ties them with a conditional connective to form a single conditional proposition. The author stresses that this process is distinct from syllogism (قياس), which is indirect inference, because direct perception does not rely on a mediating middle term but on immediate awareness of the real-world relationship. After defining the process, the author provides several examples, including: 'If daytime exists, the sun is rising,' 'Either a number is even or odd,' and 'A word is either a noun, verb, or particle.' Then he analyzes the first example in detail: we directly perceive that daytime and sunrise are concomitant, and we express this in multiple conditional statements (both directions, including negations). The key point is that these judgments are based on immediate perception of real concomitance, not syllogistic deduction. The commentator notes that the distinction between direct perception and syllogism is crucial: direct perception gives immediate awareness of connections, whereas syllogism requires a middle term and is a mediated inference.

Analyzing the First Example: Daytime and Sunrise
Detailed breakdown of the daytime-sunrise conditional example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract multiple conditional statements from a single real-world concomitant relationship and trace them back to direct perception.

Core Idea
One real-world concomitance yields many direct conditional judgments.

The author takes a common experience—the relation between daytime and sunrise—and shows how direct perception of this real concomitance generates several conditional propositions. This lesson walks through that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the perceived content
What exactly do we perceive? The author lists observations: existence of daytime, extension of brightness, conjunction with sunrise, then departure of daytime, coming of night, conjunction with sunset.
These are all sensory perceptions.
2
Recognize the concomitant relationship
After perceiving, we notice that daytime and sunrise are always together; same with night and sunset. This is a relation of 'talāzum' (concomitance).
The author calls it 'ارتباطاً تلازمياً'.
3
Formulate conditional statements
From the same real relation, we can derive multiple conditionals: both directions (if A then B, if B then A) and their negations. List all six given.
How many do you count in the text?
4
Confirm directness
Why does the author insist these are direct? Because no syllogism is needed; we see the connection immediately from experience.
Contrast with syllogism: no middle term.

اللزوم في القضايا الشرطية
من أنواع الإدراك المباشر عملية عقلية يدرك بها الناظر اللزوم في القضايا.
إن الباحث الناظر في الواقع المدرك بالحس، أو المدرك بالنظر العقلي المجرد، ليلاحظ أن قضية مثلاً مقترنة بأخرى اقتراناً غير منفك، في الوجود، أو في العدم، أو في كليهما، أو مقترن وجود إحداهما بانعدام الأخرى، أو مقترن انعدام إحداهما بوجود الأخرى، أو اقترانهما اقتران تناقض فأيهما وجد انعدم الآخر وأيهما انعدم وجد الآخر.
فيربط بين القضيتين برباط شرطي يدلّ به على ما أدركه عن الواقع لدى نظره الفكري في القضيتين، فالقضيتان معاً قد كانتا مادة الإدراك المباشر، وعندئذٍ يصدر حكماً شرطياً بما أدركه من اقتران، ويعقد بين القضيتين، ويجعل منهما قضية شرطية واحدة.
ولا تشبه هذه العملية العقلية ما يجري في القياس، لأن القياس يقدم قضية جديدة عن طريق ملاحظة شمول حكم قضية لقضية أخرى لا يوجد فيها هذا الحكم، فالقياس استدلال غير مباشر، أما الحكم باللزوم أو عدمه في القضايا فطريقه الإدراك المباشر، ما لم يستخدم لذلك القياس أو دليل آخر من أدلة الاستدلال.
إذن فمن الإدراك المباشر هذه العملية العقلية التي يدرك بها الناظر الباحث واقع اقتران بين قضيتين، فيصدر بالاستناد إليها حكماً شرطياً، فيقول مثلاً :
أ ـ إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة .
ب ـ لو تعدّدت الآلهة تعدّداً بحق لفسد نظام الكون .
جـ ـ إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً .
د ـ الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً .
هـ ـ الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل أو حرف .
و ـ كلما كان اللون أبيض فهو لا أسود .
إلى غير ذلك من أمثلة شرطية كثيرة .
فلنحلل العملية العقلية في المثال الأول :
كلنا ندرك وجود النهار وامتداد بياضه وسلامة الرؤية فيه في أغلب الأحيان، وندرك أن وجوده مقارن لطلوع الشمس وامتداد أشعتها باتجاه مكان النهار في الأرض، ثم ندرك ذهاب النهار وقدوم الليل وإقبال سواده وظلمته، وندرك أن ذهاب النهار مقارن لغروب الشمس وانحجاب أشعتها عن الأرض .
بعد هذه الإدراكات نعقد مباشرة بين قضية وجود النهار وطلوع الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً، وبين قضية وجود الليل وغروب الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً .
ونعلق كل قضية بينها وبين صاحبتها تلازم برباط شرطي، فنقول :
إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالنهار موجود .
إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة في الأفق .
إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالنهار غير موجود .
إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالليل موجود .
إذا كان الليل موجوداً فالشمس غاربة .
إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالليل غير موجود .
كل هذه القضايا أدركناها بشكل مباشر من إدراك التلازم في الواقع بين طلوع الشمس والنهار، وغروبها والليل .
Implication in conditional propositions. It is a type of direct perception: a mental process by which the observer perceives implication in propositions. Indeed, the observer investigating reality perceived by sense, or perceived by pure intellectual contemplation, notices that a proposition, for example, is conjoined with another in an inseparable conjunction, in existence, or in non-existence, or in both, or the existence of one is conjoined with the non-existence of the other, or the non-existence of one is conjoined with the existence of the other, or their conjunction is a contradictory conjunction such that whichever exists, the other ceases to exist, and whichever ceases to exist, the other exists. So he links the two propositions with a conditional connective by which he indicates what he has perceived about reality through his intellectual consideration of the two propositions. The two propositions together were the material of direct perception, and then he issues a conditional judgment based on what he perceived of conjunction, and he ties the two propositions together and makes them one conditional proposition. This mental process does not resemble what occurs in syllogism, because syllogism produces a new proposition by noting the inclusion of the judgment of one proposition in another proposition in which that judgment does not exist. So syllogism is indirect inference. As for the judgment of implication or its absence in propositions, its path is direct perception, unless syllogism or another evidence from the evidences of inference is used for that. So it is from direct perception this mental process by which the investigating observer perceives the reality of conjunction between two propositions, and then based on it he issues a conditional judgment. He says, for example: A. If daytime exists, then the sun is rising. B. If the gods were truly multiple, the system of the universe would be corrupted. C. Either the number is even or odd. D. A thing is either existent or non-existent. E. A word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle. F. Whenever a color is white, it is not black. And so on, many conditional examples. So let us analyze the mental process in the first example: All of us perceive the existence of daytime, the extension of its brightness, and the clarity of vision in it most of the time, and we perceive that its existence is concomitant with the rising of the sun and the extension of its rays towards the place of daytime on earth. Then we perceive the departure of daytime, the coming of night, the approach of its darkness and gloom, and we perceive that the departure of daytime is concomitant with the setting of the sun and the veiling of its rays from the earth. After these perceptions, we directly establish between the proposition of the existence of daytime and the rising of the sun a connection of concomitance, and between the proposition of the existence of night and the setting of the sun a connection of concomitance. And we attach each proposition to its companion with a conditional connective, saying: If the sun is rising on the horizon, then daytime exists. If daytime exists, then the sun is rising on the horizon. If the sun is setting, then daytime does not exist. If the sun is setting, then night exists. If night exists, then the sun is setting. If the sun is rising on the horizon, then night does not exist. All these propositions we perceived directly from the perception of the real concomitance between the rising of the sun and daytime, and between its setting and night.
Source pages p. 115

This passage defines the mental process of perceiving implication in conditional propositions, classifying it as a type of direct perception (إدراك مباشر). Definition of Ma'rifa (Knowledge) and Its Three Types
Author defines ma'rifa and its types by object category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify objects of knowledge into sensory, attribute-based, and abstract types, and apply the condition of correspondence to reality.

Core Idea
Knowledge is perception that must correspond to reality, taking three forms based on the object.

This passage opens the epistemological discussion by defining al-ma'rifa as the foundational perception of forms, attributes, or abstract meanings. It emphasizes that true knowledge requires the perception to match the reality of the object, and it breaks down objects into three categories. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for distinguishing different modes of knowing in Islamic intellectual sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of knowable objects
The text divides objects into: (1) those with forms perceived by external/internal senses, (2) those with attributes or characteristics not perceived by sense, and (3) abstract entities like existence, truth, goodness. Locate each in the passage.
These correspond roughly to sensible, non-sensible attributes, and metaphysical concepts.
2
Determine the mode of perception for each category
For sensible forms, knowledge is by 'imprint' in the soul; for non-sensible attributes, by grasping them as they are in reality; for abstracts, by perceiving their abstract reality. Extract these from the text.
The mode differs, but all require correspondence to the object's own reality.
3
Apply the perfection condition
The text states that perfection (kamal) of knowledge is conformity between perception and the perceived thing's reality. Test this: does each mode of perception achieve this?
This condition is universal; without it, knowledge is incomplete or false.
4
Connect to an Islamic science example
In Usul al-Fiqh, when determining a ruling, one must know whether the evidence is sensory (e.g., sighting the crescent) or based on attribute (e.g., reliability of a narrator). Categorize a given example using this taxonomy.
E.g., knowing the color of the Kaaba is sensory; knowing the attribute of 'adl (justice) in a witness is attribute-based; knowing the existence of Allah is abstract.

المَعْرِفَة وَطُرق الوُصُول إليها
المعرفة :
هي إدراك ما لصور الأشياء أو صفاتها أو سماتها وعلاماتها، أو للمعاني
المجردة سواء أكان لها في غير الذهن وجود أو لا؟ .
وكمال المعرفة يكون بمطابقة الإدراك لما عليه المدرك في واقع نفسه
من صورة أو صفة أو سمة وعلامة، أو وجود أو عدم، أو حق أو باطل، أو غير
ذلك مما يتعلق به .
فإن كان الشيء مما له صورة تُدرك بالحس الظاهر أو الباطن فمعرفة
صورته تكون بانطباع هذه الصورة في نفس المدرك، وإن كان مما له صفة ما
أو سمة ما لا تدرك بالحس، فمعرفة هذه الصفة أو هذه السمة تكون بإدراكها

والحق والباطل، والخير والشر، فمعرفته تكون بإدراك حقيقته المجردة على ما

وإدراك الشيء أو المعنى على ما هو عليه في الواقع هو ما يسمى باسم

وحين يجزم المدرك بأن ما أدركه مطابق للواقع قطعاً، ويكون كذلك في

تحيي الموتى، قال : أو لم تؤمن؟ قال : بلى ولكن ليطمئن قلبي .
Knowledge and the ways of reaching it.
Knowledge: It is the perception of the forms of things or their attributes or their characteristics and signs, or of abstract meanings, whether they have existence outside the mind or not.
The perfection of knowledge is the conformity of perception to what the perceived thing is in its own reality, whether of form, attribute, characteristic, sign, existence, non-existence, truth, falsehood, or other than that which pertains to it.
If the thing is one that has a form perceived by the external or internal sense, then knowing its form is by the imprint of this form in the soul of the perceiver. If it is something that has an attribute or characteristic not perceived by sense, then knowing this attribute or characteristic is by perceiving it as it is in reality. If it is from the intellectual abstract entities such as existence and non-existence, truth and falsehood, good and evil, then knowing it is by perceiving its abstract reality as it is in the reality of its affair. And so on to all that perception can encompass.
Science: The perception of a thing or meaning as it is in reality is what is called 'science' (al-ilm).
(1) Linguistically, 'science' may be applied to what is acquired by a predominant opinion (dhan) subject to change and modification, including in Qur'anic usage the saying of God Almighty in Surah al-Mumtahina (60:10): 'O you who have believed, when the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them. Allah is most knowing of their faith. And if you know them to be believers, do not return them to the disbelievers; they are not lawful for them, nor are they lawful for them...' So knowing their faith through examination is speculative knowledge (ilm dhan). In technical terminology, the word 'science' (al-ilm), as one of the sciences, is applied to a collection of predominant, rational pieces of knowledge, some of which are certain, provided that they are organized around a subject, such as the science of fiqh, the science of usul, the science of arithmetic, the science of geometry, the science of geography, and the science of medicine.
Certainty: When the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, and it is so in its true nature by decisive proof, then this judgment of his is called 'certainty' (al-yaqeen).
Belief: As for when the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, without his judgment being accompanied by decisive proof of its correspondence to reality, this is called 'firm belief' (al-i'tiqad al-jazim) alone or 'faith' (al-iman). This belief may not in reality correspond to the truth, in which case it is a corrupt belief.
Science may be science in one respect, certainty in another respect, and firm belief or faith in a third respect.
Degrees of science: Science that corresponds to reality has degrees, some above others, with respect to its firmness and its effect on the various aspects of the soul.
That which compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, is certainty, and it may be accompanied by faith, meaning acknowledgement and submission.
Above that is what compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, and also gives the heart complete tranquility—this is what Abraham, peace be upon him, sought when he said: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead.' He said: 'Do you not believe?' He said: 'Yes, but that my heart may be at rest.'
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction defining key terms in order. It first defines ma'rifa (knowledge) as perception of forms, attributes, or abstract meanings, emphasizing that perfection of knowledge requires correspondence between perception and reality. It then clarifies that if the object is sensible, knowledge is by impression; if non-sensible attribute, by grasping it as is; if abstract, by perceiving its reality. Next, 'ilm (science) is defined as perception of a thing as it is in reality. A lengthy footnote expands on 'ilm, noting that linguistically it can refer to speculative knowledge (dhan), citing a Qur'anic verse about examining believing women, and technically it refers to organized bodies of knowledge like fiqh, usul, etc. Then yaqeen (certainty) is defined as decisive judgment with decisive proof that the perception matches reality. Then i'tiqad (belief) is defined as decisive judgment without such proof; it may be false. The text notes that 'ilm can be classified as 'ilm, yaqeen, or i'tiqad depending on perspective. Finally, degrees of 'ilm are discussed: the lowest is yaqeen (inevitable conviction), and above that is the tranquility sought by Abraham when he asked to see how God revives the dead, indicating a higher degree where intellectual certainty is accompanied by heart's rest. The passage systematically builds from general knowledge to the highest form of certainty.

10 min
The Linguistic and Technical Meanings of 'Ilm
Author defines 'ilm and expands on its linguistic and technical meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic usage of 'ilm (which includes speculative knowledge) from the technical usage (organized sciences), and identify a Quranic example of speculative 'ilm.

Core Idea
The same word 'ilm can mean certain or speculative knowledge; the Qur'an uses it for both.

After defining ma'rifa, the passage defines 'ilm as perception of a thing as it is in reality. The author then adds a detailed footnote that expands the meaning: linguistically, 'ilm can be used for speculative knowledge (dhan), and technically, it refers to organized bodies of knowledge. This double usage is crucial for reading Islamic texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition of 'ilm
The text defines 'ilm as 'إدراك الشيء أو المعنى على ما هو عليه في الواقع' (perception of a thing or meaning as it is in reality). Note that this is identical to the definition of ma'rifa in its perfection.
This definition does not yet specify certainty; it is neutral about certitude.
2
Examine the footnote on linguistic usage
The footnote states that 'ilm can be applied to knowledge acquired by predominant opinion (dhan), which is subject to change. It gives the Quranic example from Surah al-Mumtahina: 'if you know them to be believers' – where knowing is based on examination, not certainty.
This shows that in Arabic, 'ilm can be used for speculative knowledge, contrary to later technical restrictions.
3
Understand the technical usage
The footnote also explains that technically, 'ilm (as a science) is a collection of predominant rational pieces of knowledge (some certain) organized around a subject, e.g., fiqh, usul, medicine.
This technical sense focuses on organized knowledge, not necessarily all certain.
4
Apply to Islamic studies
When reading a fiqh manual, the term 'ilm may refer to the organized discipline (fiqh itself) or to a particular certain ruling. Distinguish based on context.
E.g., 'ilm al-fiqh is the science; 'ilm al-hukm is knowledge of a ruling.

المَعْرِفَة وَطُرق الوُصُول إليها
المعرفة :
هي إدراك ما لصور الأشياء أو صفاتها أو سماتها وعلاماتها، أو للمعاني
المجردة سواء أكان لها في غير الذهن وجود أو لا؟ .
وكمال المعرفة يكون بمطابقة الإدراك لما عليه المدرك في واقع نفسه
من صورة أو صفة أو سمة وعلامة، أو وجود أو عدم، أو حق أو باطل، أو غير
ذلك مما يتعلق به .
فإن كان الشيء مما له صورة تُدرك بالحس الظاهر أو الباطن فمعرفة
صورته تكون بانطباع هذه الصورة في نفس المدرك، وإن كان مما له صفة ما
أو سمة ما لا تدرك بالحس، فمعرفة هذه الصفة أو هذه السمة تكون بإدراكها
على ما هي عليه في الواقع، وإن كان من المجردات العقلية كالوجود والعدم،
والحق والباطل، والخير والشر، فمعرفته تكون بإدراك حقيقته المجردة على ما
هي عليه في واقع أمرها، وهكذا إلى سائر ما يمكن أن يتناوله الإدراك .
العلم :
وإدراك الشيء أو المعنى على ما هو عليه في الواقع هو ما يسمى باسم
(العلم)(۱).
(۱) قد يُطلق «العلم» لغة على المكتسب بدليل ظني راجح قابل للتغيير والتعديل، ومنه في
الاستعمالات القرآنية قول الله عزّ وجلّ في سورة (الممتحنة، ٦) : ﴿ يا أيها الذين آمنوا إذا
جَاءَكُمُ المؤمناتُ مهاجراتٍ فامتحنوهنَّ - الله أعلم بإيمانهن - فإن علمتموهنَّ مؤمناتٍ فلا
ترجعوهنَّ إلى الكفار لا هنَّ حلّ لهم ولا هم يحلّون لهن . . . . ) فالعلم بإيمانهن عن طريق
الامتحان علم ظني .
ويطلق في الاصطلاح لفظ (العلم)، واحد العلوم، على مجموعة معارف ظنية راجحة ومنها
ما هو قطعي، بشرط أن تكون منظمة حول موضوع ما . كعلم الفقه، وعلم الأصول، وعلم
الحساب، وعلم الهندسة، وعلم الجغرافيا، وعلم الطب .
اليقين :
وحين يجزم المدرك بأن ما أدركه مطابق للواقع قطعاً، ويكون كذلك في
حقيقة أمره بالدليل القاطع فإن جزمه هذا هو ما يسمى باسم (اليقين).
الاعتقاد :
أما حين يجزم المدرك بأن ما أدركه مطابق للواقع قطعاً، دون أن يقترن
جزمه بالدليل القاطع على مطابقته للواقع، فهو ما يطلق عليه اسم «الاعتقاد
الجازم» فقط أو اسم «الإيمان»، وقد لا يكون هذا الاعتقاد في حقيقة الأمر
مطابقاً للواقع وعندئذ يكون اعتقاداً فاسداً .
والعلم قد يكون علماً باعتبار، ويقيناً باعتبار آخر، واعتقاداً جازماً أو
إيماناً باعتبار ثالث .
درجات العلم :
والعلم المطابق للواقع على درجات بعضها فوق بعض بالنسبة إلى
تمكنه وتأثيره على جوانب النفس المختلفة .
فما يلزم الفكر إلزاماً لا يحتمل النقيض فهو اليقين، وقد يصاحبه
الإيمان، بمعنى الاعتراف والتسليم .
وفوق ذلك ما يلزم الفكر إلزاماً لا يحتمل النقيض ويورث القلب مع
ذلك طمأنينة تامة، وهو ما طلبه إبراهيم عليه السلام إذ قال : رب أرني كيف
تحيي الموتى، قال : أو لم تؤمن؟ قال : بلى ولكن ليطمئن قلبي .
Knowledge and the ways of reaching it.
Knowledge: It is the perception of the forms of things or their attributes or their characteristics and signs, or of abstract meanings, whether they have existence outside the mind or not.
The perfection of knowledge is the conformity of perception to what the perceived thing is in its own reality, whether of form, attribute, characteristic, sign, existence, non-existence, truth, falsehood, or other than that which pertains to it.
If the thing is one that has a form perceived by the external or internal sense, then knowing its form is by the imprint of this form in the soul of the perceiver. If it is something that has an attribute or characteristic not perceived by sense, then knowing this attribute or characteristic is by perceiving it as it is in reality. If it is from the intellectual abstract entities such as existence and non-existence, truth and falsehood, good and evil, then knowing it is by perceiving its abstract reality as it is in the reality of its affair. And so on to all that perception can encompass.
Science: The perception of a thing or meaning as it is in reality is what is called 'science' (al-ilm).
(1) Linguistically, 'science' may be applied to what is acquired by a predominant opinion (dhan) subject to change and modification, including in Qur'anic usage the saying of God Almighty in Surah al-Mumtahina (60:10): 'O you who have believed, when the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them. Allah is most knowing of their faith. And if you know them to be believers, do not return them to the disbelievers; they are not lawful for them, nor are they lawful for them...' So knowing their faith through examination is speculative knowledge (ilm dhan). In technical terminology, the word 'science' (al-ilm), as one of the sciences, is applied to a collection of predominant, rational pieces of knowledge, some of which are certain, provided that they are organized around a subject, such as the science of fiqh, the science of usul, the science of arithmetic, the science of geometry, the science of geography, and the science of medicine.
Certainty: When the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, and it is so in its true nature by decisive proof, then this judgment of his is called 'certainty' (al-yaqeen).
Belief: As for when the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, without his judgment being accompanied by decisive proof of its correspondence to reality, this is called 'firm belief' (al-i'tiqad al-jazim) alone or 'faith' (al-iman). This belief may not in reality correspond to the truth, in which case it is a corrupt belief.
Science may be science in one respect, certainty in another respect, and firm belief or faith in a third respect.
Degrees of science: Science that corresponds to reality has degrees, some above others, with respect to its firmness and its effect on the various aspects of the soul.
That which compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, is certainty, and it may be accompanied by faith, meaning acknowledgement and submission.
Above that is what compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, and also gives the heart complete tranquility—this is what Abraham, peace be upon him, sought when he said: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead.' He said: 'Do you not believe?' He said: 'Yes, but that my heart may be at rest.'
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction defining key terms in order. It first defines ma'rifa (knowledge) as perception of forms, attributes, or abstract meanings, emphasizing that perfection of knowledge requires correspondence between perception and reality. It then clarifies that if the object is sensible, knowledge is by impression; if non-sensible attribute, by grasping it as is; if abstract, by perceiving its reality. Next, 'ilm (science) is defined as perception of a thing as it is in reality. A lengthy footnote expands on 'ilm, noting that linguistically it can refer to speculative knowledge (dhan), citing a Qur'anic verse about examining believing women, and technically it refers to organized bodies of knowledge like fiqh, usul, etc. Then yaqeen (certainty) is defined as decisive judgment with decisive proof that the perception matches reality. Then i'tiqad (belief) is defined as decisive judgment without such proof; it may be false. The text notes that 'ilm can be classified as 'ilm, yaqeen, or i'tiqad depending on perspective. Finally, degrees of 'ilm are discussed: the lowest is yaqeen (inevitable conviction), and above that is the tranquility sought by Abraham when he asked to see how God revives the dead, indicating a higher degree where intellectual certainty is accompanied by heart's rest. The passage systematically builds from general knowledge to the highest form of certainty.

العلم الظني
العلم القطعي
العلم الاصطلاحي
العلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, what is the Quranic example of 'ilm used for speculative knowledge?
A) The knowledge of Allah's essence.
B) The knowledge that the world is created.
C) The knowledge that believing women are believers after examination.
D) The knowledge of the unseen.

Key Takeaway
The term 'ilm is used in both a broad sense (including speculative knowledge) and a technical sense (organized science); context determines which meaning applies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the Quranic verse in the footnote (Q 60:10). Explain why the 'ilm mentioned there is considered speculative (dhanni) rather than certain (qat'i).
?
Give an example from fiqh where a ruling is based on 'ilm dhanni and explain why it is not certain.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
10 min
Yaqeen, I'tiqad, and Degrees of Knowledge
Author defines yaqeen and i'tiqad and discusses degrees of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish yaqeen from i'tiqad based on the presence of decisive proof, and rank the degrees of knowledge from intellectual certainty to heart's tranquility.

Core Idea
Certainty requires decisive proof; belief without proof may be false; the highest knowledge brings both intellectual compulsion and heart's rest.

The passage now defines yaqeen and i'tiqad, two critical concepts in Islamic epistemology. Yaqeen is decisive judgment with decisive proof; i'tiqad is decisive judgment without such proof. The same 'ilm can be viewed as any of these depending on the presence of proof. Finally, the passage outlines degrees of knowledge, culminating in the tranquility of the heart illustrated by Prophet Ibrahim's request.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definitions of yaqeen and i'tiqad
Yaqeen: decisive judgment (jazm) that perception matches reality, supported by decisive proof (dalil qati'). I'tiqad: decisive judgment without decisive proof. Locate these in the text.
The key difference is the presence or absence of decisive proof.
2
Note the relationship between 'ilm, yaqeen, and i'tiqad
The text says 'ilm may be considered 'ilm in one respect, yaqeen in another, and i'tiqad in another. This means the same mental state can be categorized differently depending on whether proof is present.
This shows flexibility: e.g., knowledge of Allah's existence is 'ilm, but if one has decisive proof, it becomes yaqeen; if one believes without proof, it is i'tiqad.
3
Analyze the degrees of knowledge
First degree: yaqeen – compels the intellect inevitably. Second degree: something above yaqeen that also gives the heart complete tranquility (tuma'nina). The text cites Prophet Ibrahim's request: 'Rabbi arini kayfa tuhyi al-mawta... li yatma'ina qalbi.'
This shows that knowledge can have different effects on the soul; the highest level affects both intellect and heart.
4
Apply to a theological example
Consider belief in the resurrection. A person may have yaqeen based on Quranic proofs, but the highest degree is when that belief also produces complete tranquility in the heart.
This is the state Ibrahim (as) sought.

Knowledge and the ways of reaching it.
Knowledge: It is the perception of the forms of things or their attributes or their characteristics and signs, or of abstract meanings, whether they have existence outside the mind or not.
The perfection of knowledge is the conformity of perception to what the perceived thing is in its own reality, whether of form, attribute, characteristic, sign, existence, non-existence, truth, falsehood, or other than that which pertains to it.
If the thing is one that has a form perceived by the external or internal sense, then knowing its form is by the imprint of this form in the soul of the perceiver. If it is something that has an attribute or characteristic not perceived by sense, then knowing this attribute or characteristic is by perceiving it as it is in reality. If it is from the intellectual abstract entities such as existence and non-existence, truth and falsehood, good and evil, then knowing it is by perceiving its abstract reality as it is in the reality of its affair. And so on to all that perception can encompass.
Science: The perception of a thing or meaning as it is in reality is what is called 'science' (al-ilm).
(1) Linguistically, 'science' may be applied to what is acquired by a predominant opinion (dhan) subject to change and modification, including in Qur'anic usage the saying of God Almighty in Surah al-Mumtahina (60:10): 'O you who have believed, when the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them. Allah is most knowing of their faith. And if you know them to be believers, do not return them to the disbelievers; they are not lawful for them, nor are they lawful for them...' So knowing their faith through examination is speculative knowledge (ilm dhan). In technical terminology, the word 'science' (al-ilm), as one of the sciences, is applied to a collection of predominant, rational pieces of knowledge, some of which are certain, provided that they are organized around a subject, such as the science of fiqh, the science of usul, the science of arithmetic, the science of geometry, the science of geography, and the science of medicine.
Certainty: When the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, and it is so in its true nature by decisive proof, then this judgment of his is called 'certainty' (al-yaqeen).
Belief: As for when the perceiver decisively judges that what he perceives corresponds to reality absolutely, without his judgment being accompanied by decisive proof of its correspondence to reality, this is called 'firm belief' (al-i'tiqad al-jazim) alone or 'faith' (al-iman). This belief may not in reality correspond to the truth, in which case it is a corrupt belief.
Science may be science in one respect, certainty in another respect, and firm belief or faith in a third respect.
Degrees of science: Science that corresponds to reality has degrees, some above others, with respect to its firmness and its effect on the various aspects of the soul.
That which compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, is certainty, and it may be accompanied by faith, meaning acknowledgement and submission.
Above that is what compels the intellect inevitably, not admitting the contradictory, and also gives the heart complete tranquility—this is what Abraham, peace be upon him, sought when he said: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead.' He said: 'Do you not believe?' He said: 'Yes, but that my heart may be at rest.'

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction defining key terms in order. It first defines ma'rifa (knowledge) as perception of forms, attributes, or abstract meanings, emphasizing that perfection of knowledge requires correspondence between perception and reality. It then clarifies that if the object is sensible, knowledge is by impression; if non-sensible attribute, by grasping it as is; if abstract, by perceiving its reality. Next, 'ilm (science) is defined as perception of a thing as it is in reality. A lengthy footnote expands on 'ilm, noting that linguistically it can refer to speculative knowledge (dhan), citing a Qur'anic verse about examining believing women, and technically it refers to organized bodies of knowledge like fiqh, usul, etc. Then yaqeen (certainty) is defined as decisive judgment with decisive proof that the perception matches reality. Then i'tiqad (belief) is defined as decisive judgment without such proof; it may be false. The text notes that 'ilm can be classified as 'ilm, yaqeen, or i'tiqad depending on perspective. Finally, degrees of 'ilm are discussed: the lowest is yaqeen (inevitable conviction), and above that is the tranquility sought by Abraham when he asked to see how God revives the dead, indicating a higher degree where intellectual certainty is accompanied by heart's rest. The passage systematically builds from general knowledge to the highest form of certainty.

According to the text, what distinguishes the highest degree of knowledge from mere yaqeen?
A) It is based on sensory experience.

Key Takeaway

?

?
Read the story of Prophet Ibrahim in the Quran (2:260). According to the text, what degree of knowledge did he already have, and what higher degree did he seek? How does this relate to 'tuma'nina'?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
Yaqin: Certainty with Decisive Proof
Author defines yaqin as certain knowledge with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define yaqin and identify its two essential components: decisive judgment and decisive proof.

Core Idea
Certainty is a judgment matching reality, confirmed by decisive proof.

The passage defines yaqin as a firm judgment that what is perceived corresponds to reality, and this correspondence is proven by decisive proof. This sets the highest epistemic standard in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate of the definition
The subject is 'al-mudrik' (the perceiver) who judges. The predicate is that the judgment corresponds to reality decisively.
2
Note the two conditions
First, the judgment is decisive (jazm). Second, it is backed by decisive proof (dalil qati') that the correspondence is true in reality.
3
Recognize the dual nature of yaqin
Yaqin has both a subjective component (firm belief) and an objective component (proof-based correctness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليقين :
وحين يجزم المدرك بأن ما أدركه مطابق للواقع قطعاً، ويكون كذلك في
حقيقة أمره بالدليل القاطع فإن جزمه هذا هو ما يسمى باسم (اليقين).
الاعتقاد :
أما حين يجزم المدرك بأن ما أدركه مطابق للواقع قطعاً، دون أن يقترن
جزمه بالدليل القاطع على مطابقته للواقع، فهو ما يطلق عليه اسم «الاعتقاد
الجازم» فقط أو اسم «الإيمان»، وقد لا يكون هذا الاعتقاد في حقيقة الأمر
مطابقاً للواقع وعندئذ يكون اعتقاداً فاسداً .
والعلم قد يكون علماً باعتبار، ويقيناً باعتبار آخر، واعتقاداً جازماً أو
إيماناً باعتبار ثالث .
Certainty: When the perceiver judges that what he has perceived corresponds to reality decisively, and it is indeed that way in its true nature by decisive proof, then this judgment of his is called 'certainty' (yaqin). Belief: As for when the perceiver judges that what he has perceived corresponds to reality decisively, without his judgment being accompanied by decisive proof of its correspondence to reality, then it is called 'firm belief' (i'tiqad jazim) or 'faith' (iman), and this belief may not in reality correspond to reality, in which case it is a corrupt belief. Knowledge may be knowledge from one perspective, certainty from another, and firm belief or faith from a third perspective.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines two key epistemic categories: yaqin (certainty) and i'tiqad (belief). The author begins by defining yaqin: it is a judgment (jazm) that the perceiver's mental content corresponds to reality, AND this judgment is backed by qati' dalil (decisive proof) that confirms that the correspondence is real. So yaqin has both subjective and objective components. Next, i'tiqad is defined: it is also a firm judgment about correspondence, but without decisive proof. It is called 'firm belief' or 'faith'. Importantly, such belief may be mistaken (corrupt). The passage then notes that 'ilm (knowledge) can be considered differently: from one aspect it is knowledge, from another certainty, and from a third it is belief. This sets the stage for a hierarchy of epistemic states.

Key Terms
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two necessary components of yaqin?
A) Decisive judgment and decisive proof
B) Decisive judgment and subjective certainty
C) Proof and unanimity
D) Judgment and correspondence

Key Takeaway
Yaqin is a firm judgment proven by decisive proof, ensuring its correspondence to reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a piece of Islamic knowledge that you hold with yaqin (e.g., the obligation of prayer) and explain how it meets both criteria of yaqin according to this definition.

I'tiqad: Firm Belief Without Proof
Author defines i'tiqad as firm belief without proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define i'tiqad and distinguish it from yaqin by the absence of decisive proof.

Core Idea
Firm belief is a judgment without decisive proof; it can be true or false.

The passage then defines i'tiqad as a firm judgment about correspondence to reality, but without decisive proof. This makes it distinct from yaqin and opens the possibility of error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
I'tiqad is a judgment (jazm) that something corresponds to reality decisively, but without proof.
2
Note the alternative names
It is also called 'i'tiqad jazim' (firm belief) or 'iman' (faith).
3
Understand the possibility of error
I'tiqad may be correct or corrupt (fasid) depending on actual correspondence.
4
Contrast with yaqin
Yaqin always corresponds to reality because of proof; i'tiqad may not, due to lack of proof.

Source Text

Three Degrees of Certain Knowledge
Author describes three ascending degrees of certain knowledge: logical necessity, heart tranquility, and complete pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three degrees of certain knowledge and identify how each engages the intellect, heart, and soul.

Core Idea
Certainty deepens from intellectual necessity to heart tranquility to soul-pleasure.

This passage presents a hierarchy of certain knowledge. While many recognize that knowledge must correspond to reality, the author shows that such knowledge admits of degrees in its firmness and its impact on the human person. Understanding these degrees helps us appreciate why the Qur'an distinguishes between intellectual belief and heart certainty.

Guided Path
1
Name the three degrees
Identify the Arabic terms for each degree: al-yaqin, al-tuma'nina, and al-mut'a wa al-ladhdha. Note that each builds on the previous.
2
Associate each with a faculty
Yaqin primarily engages the intellect ('aql); tuma'nina adds the heart (qalb); the highest degree engages the soul (nafs) as well, unifying all inner faculties.
3
Examine the Prophet Ibrahim example
The verse shows Ibrahim already had yaqin ('Do you not believe?' 'Yes') but sought tuma'nina. This illustrates the second degree.
4
Recognize the practical outcome
The highest degree leads to unified inner motivation, enabling consistent righteous action without internal conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
درجات العلم :
والعلم المطابق للواقع على درجات بعضها فوق بعض بالنسبة إلى
تمكنه وتأثيره على جوانب النفس المختلفة .
فما يلزم الفكر إلزاماً لا يحتمل النقيض فهو اليقين، وقد يصاحبه
الإيمان، بمعنى الاعتراف والتسليم .
وفوق ذلك ما يلزم الفكر إلزاماً لا يحتمل النقيض ويورث القلب مع
ذلك طمأنينة تامة، وهو ما طلبه إبراهيم عليه السلام إذ قال : رب أرني كيف
تحيي الموتى، قال : أو لم تؤمن؟ قال : بلى ولكن ليطمئن قلبي .
وفوقهما ما يلزم الفكر ويورث القلب طمأنينة تامة، ويورث النفس مع
ذلك متعة ولذة، وعندئذ يملك كل أركان الإنسان الداخلية: (فكره وقلبه
ونفسه) وعندئذ يكون قادراً على توجيه سلوك الإنسان دون معارض من داخل
نفسه .
Degrees of Knowledge: Knowledge that corresponds to reality has degrees, some above others in terms of its firmness and its effect on the various aspects of the soul. What compels the intellect in a way that does not admit the opposite is certainty (yaqin), and it may be accompanied by faith (iman), meaning acknowledgment and submission. Above that is what compels the intellect in a way that does not admit the opposite and also bestows on the heart complete tranquility (tuma'nina), and that is what Ibrahim, peace be upon him, asked for when he said: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead.' He said: 'Do you not believe?' He said: 'Yes, but so that my heart may be at ease.' Above both is what compels the intellect, bestows on the heart complete tranquility, and also gives the soul pleasure and delight, and then it takes possession of all the inner pillars of the human being: his intellect, his heart, and his soul, and then it is able to direct the behavior of the human without opposition from within himself.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage classifies true knowledge ('ilm mutabiq li al-waqi') into three ascending degrees based on its firmness and influence on the soul. The first degree is yaqin: it logically compels the intellect such that the opposite is impossible, and may be accompanied by simple faith (i'tiqad or iman). The second degree is higher: it also logically compels the intellect and additionally brings heart tranquility (tuma'nina). The author cites the Qur'anic story of Prophet Ibrahim asking to see how God revives the dead, not because he doubted, but to achieve heart contentment. The third and highest degree goes further: it gives the soul pleasure (mut'a) and delight (ladhdha), engaging all inner faculties—intellect, heart, and soul—so that the person is internally unified and can act without inner conflict. This hierarchy reflects the depth of conviction and its impact on the whole person.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqin): The first degree of certain knowledge: logical necessity that excludes the opposite, often accompanied by simple faith. [Note: Here yaqin is the first degree; in other contexts it may cover all certainty.]
الطمأنينة (al-tuma'nina): Heart tranquility that accompanies certainty, making the heart at ease beyond mere intellectual assent. [Note: Common in Sufi and kalam works; here it is an epistemic quality.]
المتعة واللذة (al-mut'a wa al-ladhdha): Pleasure and delight that the soul experiences from the highest degree of certainty. [Note: Not typical in logic texts; this is more psychological.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead.'
B) 'Do they not reflect on the Qur'an?'
C) 'And your Lord is the Most Generous.'
D) 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons.'

Key Takeaway
Certain knowledge admits three ascending degrees: intellectual necessity, heart tranquility, and soul-pleasure, each engaging deeper layers of the self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the hadith 'Actions are according to intentions.' Classify the certainty of this hadith using the three degrees. Which degree does a person who accepts it intellectually but feels no heart tranquility fall under?
