Epistemic States Below Certainty: Zann, Shakk, Wahm
Author classifies dominant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion as degrees below certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dominant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion based on their definitions and degrees, and apply these distinctions to Islamic juristic reasoning.

Core Idea
Below certainty, there are three graded states: dominant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion.

In this lesson, we explore the author's classification of epistemic states that fall below the rank of certainty (yaqin). These include dominant opinion (al-zann al-rajih), doubt (al-shakk), and weak opinion (al-zann al-marjuh, also called wahm). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating evidence in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three epistemic states
The passage explicitly names three states below certainty: dominant opinion (zann rajih), doubt (shakk), and weak opinion (zann marjuh). Note that weak opinion is also called wahm (delusion).
2
Define each state based on preponderance
Dominant opinion has one side stronger; doubt has completely equal probabilities; weak opinion has the opposite side stronger. The author emphasizes that doubt is perception without preponderance.
These definitions rely on the concept of tarjih (preponderance).
3
Note the degrees within dominant and weak opinion
Dominant opinion has degrees from the highest (mashhur, approaching certainty) down to the lowest (bordering doubt). Weak opinion has corresponding degrees in reverse, based on the strength of the opposite probability.
The mashhur is exemplified by common moral judgments like justice and virtues.
4
Recognize linguistic range of terms
Footnotes clarify that both 'zann' and 'shakk' can linguistically extend to weaker states, including weak opinion. For example, the Qur'anic verse 'Avoid much assumption' uses 'zann' broadly, and 'Is there any doubt about Allah?' uses 'shakk' to imply weak opinion.
This linguistic breadth is important for interpreting religious texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظن الراجح :
ودون مرتبة اليقين تأتي مرتبة الظن الراجح، والظن(۱) الراجح درجات:
أعلاها (المشهور) وهو الذي يقارب اليقين، حتى لا يكاد يخطر على
الفكر أن نقيضه ربما كان ممكناً، كأكثر قضايا العدل، والفضائل، والرذائل.
وتتنازل الدرجات حتى تنتهي بأدناها، وهي التي ليس بينها وبين الشك
درجة .
(۱) يطلق لفظ «الظن» لغة على كل درجات ما دون العلم اليقيني حتى أدنى درجات الوهم، ومنه
في الاستعمالات القرآنية: ﴿ اجتنبوا كثيراً من الظن إن بعض الظن إثم ﴾ . ولا يكون الظن
إثماً إلا إذا كان مما لا يصح الاعتماد عليه في الأحكام والأقضية والاجتهادات .
الشك :
وتأتي من دون مرتبة الظن الراجح مرتبة الشك، وهي مرتبة تتساوى فيها
الاحتمالات تساوياً تاماً، فلا يكون لبعضها رجحان على بعض، والإدراك في
هذه المرتبة إدراك بلا رجحان(۲).
(۲) يستعمل في اللغة «الشك» بهذا المعنى، وبما دونه حتى أدنى الوهم من الظنون المرجوحة،
ومنه في الاستعمالات القرآنية: ﴿ أفي الله شك ؟ ﴾ أي : أفي تحقق وجوده أدنى ظن
مرجوح ؟ .
الظن المرجوح :
وتأتي من دون مرتبة الشك مرتبة الظن المرجوح، وهو الظن الوهمي
المقابل للظن الراجح، ولذلك يسمى (وهماً)، وهو على درجات تقابل
درجات الظن الراجح .
فبمقدار رجحان الاحتمال المقابل له تكون نسبة ضعفه، فإذا كان
Dominant Opinion (al-zann al-rajih): Below the rank of certainty comes the rank of dominant opinion, and dominant opinion has degrees. Its highest is the well-known (mashhur), which approaches certainty such that its opposite hardly ever occurs to the mind as possible, like most issues of justice, virtues, and vices. The degrees descend until they end at its lowest, which has no degree between it and doubt. (1) The word 'zann' linguistically applies to all degrees below certain knowledge down to the lowest degrees of delusion (wahm), including in Qur'anic usage: 'Avoid much assumption; indeed some assumption is sin.' Assumption is only sinful if it is not reliable in rulings, judgments, and juristic reasoning. Doubt (al-shakk): Below the rank of dominant opinion comes the rank of doubt, which is a rank where probabilities are completely equal, so none outweighs another, and perception in this rank is perception without preponderance. (2) In language, 'shakk' is used in this sense and also for what is below it down to the lowest delusion from among weak opinions, including in Qur'anic usage: 'Is there any doubt about Allah?' meaning: is there the slightest weak opinion about His existence? Weak Opinion (al-zann al-marjuh): Below the rank of doubt comes the rank of weak opinion, which is the delusional opinion corresponding to dominant opinion, and therefore it is called 'delusion' (wahm). It has degrees corresponding to the degrees of dominant opinion. The extent of its weakness is proportional to the preponderance of its opposite probability, so if...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage describes three epistemic states below certainty: dominant opinion (zann rajih), doubt (shakk), and weak opinion (zann marjuh, also called wahm). The author starts with dominant opinion, noting it has degrees; the highest is 'mashhur' (well-known), which nearly equals certainty in strength (e.g., common moral judgments). The lowest degree of dominant opinion directly borders doubt. A footnote clarifies that the term 'zann' linguistically covers all sub-certain states, including weak opinion, and cites a Qur'anic verse warning against sinful assumption. Next is doubt (shakk), defined as equal probability on both sides, with perception having no preponderance. A footnote notes that linguistically 'shakk' can also include weak opinion, as in the Qur'anic rhetorical question about doubt in God. Finally, weak opinion (zann marjuh) is introduced: it is the opposite of dominant opinion, also called 'wahm' (delusion). Its degrees mirror those of dominant opinion but in reverse, with weakness proportional to the strength of the opposite probable view. The text cuts off mid-sentence explaining this proportion, but the structure is clear.

الشك
الظن المرجوح
الوهم
المشهور

Key Takeaway
The classification of sub-certain epistemic states into dominant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion provides a precise framework for evaluating knowledge claims in Islamic sciences.

Predominant Opinion and Its Degrees
Definition and degrees of predominant opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rank of predominant opinion (al-żann al-rājiḥ) and distinguish its highest degree (al-mashhūr) from the lowest degree adjacent to doubt.

Core Idea
Predominant opinion has degrees from near-certainty to the threshold of doubt.

The author places predominant opinion (al-żann al-rājiḥ) just below certainty. It is not a single level but a spectrum. The highest degree, al-mashhūr, is so strong that its opposite is hardly conceivable – similar to how we treat matters of justice. The lowest degree is barely stronger than doubt. Understanding this gradation helps us evaluate arguments that rely on probable evidence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of predominant opinion
Find where the author says 'الظن الراجح : ودون مرتبة اليقين تأتي مرتبة الظن الراجح'. Note that it is below certainty.
2
Identify the highest degree: al-mashhūr
The author says 'أعلاها (المشهور) وهو الذي يقارب اليقين'. This degree is so strong that the opposite is rarely considered possible. Think of universal ethical truths like 'justice is good'.
al-mashhūr literally means 'well-known' but here it is a technical term.
3
Trace the descending degrees
The degrees descend gradually until the lowest degree is 'التي ليس بينها وبين الشك درجة'. That means it is just above doubt.
4
Check the boundary with doubt
Verify that the lowest degree of predominant opinion is separated from doubt by no other degree; the next lower rank is doubt itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفوقهما ما يلزم الفكر ويورث القلب طمأنينة تامة، ويورث النفس مع ذلك متعة ولذة، وعندئذ يملك كل أركان الإنسان الداخلية: (فكره وقلبه ونفسه) وعندئذ يكون قادراً على توجيه سلوك الإنسان دون معارض من داخل نفسه . الظن الراجح : ودون مرتبة اليقين تأتي مرتبة الظن الراجح، والظن(۱) الراجح درجات: أعلاها (المشهور) وهو الذي يقارب اليقين، حتى لا يكاد يخطر على الفكر أن نقيضه ربما كان ممكناً، كأكثر قضايا العدل، والفضائل، والرذائل. وتتنازل الدرجات حتى تنتهي بأدناها، وهي التي ليس بينها وبين الشك درجة . الشك : وتأتي من دون مرتبة الظن الراجح مرتبة الشك، وهي مرتبة تتساوى فيها الاحتمالات تساوياً تاماً، فلا يكون لبعضها رجحان على بعض، والإدراك في هذه المرتبة إدراك بلا رجحان(۲). الظن المرجوح : وتأتي من دون مرتبة الشك مرتبة الظن المرجوح، وهو الظن الوهمي المقابل للظن الراجح، ولذلك يسمى (وهماً)، وهو على درجات تقابل درجات الظن الراجح . فبمقدار رجحان الاحتمال المقابل له تكون نسبة ضعفه، فإذا كان (۱) يطلق لفظ «الظن» لغة على كل درجات ما دون العلم اليقيني حتى أدنى درجات الوهم، ومنه في الاستعمالات القرآنية: ﴿ اجتنبوا كثيراً من الظن إن بعض الظن إثم ﴾ . ولا يكون الظن إثماً إلا إذا كان مما لا يصح الاعتماد عليه في الأحكام والأقضية والاجتهادات . (۲) يستعمل في اللغة «الشك» بهذا المعنى، وبما دونه حتى أدنى الوهم من الظنون المرجوحة، ومنه في الاستعمالات القرآنية: ﴿ أفي الله شك ؟ ﴾ أي : أفي تحقق وجوده أدنى ظن مرجوح ؟ . ١٢٥ مرتبة ما دون الظن المرجوح: وليس دون مرتبة الظن المرجوح إلا مرتبة الباطل بيقين، وعندئذ يدخل في عموم اليقين، وتقفل الدائرة. وحين يصل الظن المرجوح إلى مرتبة الباطل بيقين يصطدم بسقف الطيش، كما يصطدم نقيضه بأرضية الحق بيقين، ويمثله الرسم البياني في الشكل الأول التالي، أو يصير أفقي الميزان قائماً، ويمثله الرسم البياني في الشكل الثاني التالي: الشكل الأول سقف الطيش وإليه ينتهي الباطل بيقين درجات الظن المرجوح "الوهم" الشك الشك درجات الظن الراجح أرضية الرجحان وعندها يستقر الحق بيقين الشكل الثاني الشك الشك ظن راجح ظن مرجوح " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " باطل بيقين حق بيقين طرق الوصول إلى المعرفة: عملية البحث عن المعرفة عملية تعاون فيها وسائل الحس الظاهرة والباطنة والآلات والأدوات التي تستخدمها الحواس، وموازين العقل الفطرية ١٢٦
And above them two is what necessitates thought and bequeaths to the heart complete tranquility, and bequeaths to the soul along with that pleasure and delight, and then it possesses all the inner pillars of the human being: (his thought, his heart, and his soul), and then he is able to direct human behavior without opposition from within himself. Predominant opinion: And below the rank of certainty comes the rank of predominant opinion, and predominant opinion has degrees: its highest is (the well-known), which is close to certainty, such that it hardly occurs to the mind that its opposite could be possible, like most matters of justice, virtues, and vices. And the degrees descend until they end at its lowest, which is the one between which and doubt there is no degree. Doubt: And below the rank of predominant opinion comes the rank of doubt, and it is a rank in which probabilities are completely equal, so none of them outweighs another, and perception in this rank is perception without preponderance. Weak opinion: And below the rank of doubt comes the rank of weak opinion, and it is the illusory opinion opposite to predominant opinion, and therefore it is called (illusion), and it has degrees corresponding to the degrees of predominant opinion. For as much as the preponderance of the opposite probability, so is its weakness; if the opposite probability is close to certainty of affirmation, then it is close to certainty of negation, and if it is below that descending towards the rank of doubt, then it ascends towards the rank of doubt as well until they approximate, as if they are on two pans of a balance scale; whenever one of them increases in preponderance, its opposite increases in lightness and weakness, and whenever one of them decreases in preponderance, its opposite increases in its approximation to it. The rank below weak opinion: There is nothing below the rank of weak opinion except the rank of certain falsehood, and then it enters into the generality of certainty, and the circle closes. And when weak opinion reaches the rank of certain falsehood, it hits the ceiling of frivolity, just as its opposite hits the floor of certain truth, and the bar chart represents it in the following first figure, or the horizontal of the scale becomes vertical, and the bar chart represents it in the following second figure: First figure Ceiling of frivolity and to it ends certain falsehood Degrees of weak opinion "illusion" Doubt Doubt Degrees of predominant opinion Floor of preponderance and at it rests certain truth Second figure Doubt Doubt Predominant opinion Weak opinion " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " Certain falsehood Certain truth Ways to attain knowledge: The process of seeking knowledge is a process in which cooperate the external and internal senses, the instruments and tools that the senses use, and the innate scales of the intellect.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage continues a classification of degrees of certainty, focusing on levels below the highest certainty. The author begins by referring to two previously mentioned levels ('above them two') — likely the highest degrees of certainty or immediate knowledge — and describes a state that gives complete tranquility to the heart and soul, enabling one to control one's behavior. Then the author defines the lower levels in order: 1) Predominant opinion (al-żann al-rājiḥ): this rank is below certainty but still close; it is divided into degrees, the highest being 'al-mashhūr' (well-known), which is so close to certainty that its opposite is hardly conceivable (e.g., most judgments about justice, virtues, vices). The degrees descend until the lowest degree is adjacent to doubt. 2) Doubt (al-shakk): below predominant opinion, where probabilities are exactly equal, so no side outweighs the other. Perception here has no preponderance. 3) Weak opinion (al-żann al-marjūḥ): below doubt, it is the opposite of predominant opinion, also called 'illusion' (wahm). It has degrees that correspond inversely to the degrees of predominant opinion: as the opposite probability increases, this weakens proportionally. The author uses the analogy of a balance scale: as one side becomes heavier, the other becomes lighter. 4) Below weak opinion is only certain falsehood (al-bāṭil bi-yaqīn), which then falls under 'certainty' in a broad sense, completing the circle. At the extreme, weak opinion hits the 'ceiling of frivolity' while its opposite (certain truth) rests on the 'floor of preponderance'. The author mentions two diagrams (shown in the text) to illustrate this. Finally, the author transitions to a new section on the ways to attain knowledge, emphasizing that it involves cooperation of external and internal senses, instruments, and the innate faculties of the intellect. The passage is primarily descriptive and classificatory, aiming to map the epistemological spectrum from certainty to falsehood.

Key Terms
الظن الراجح
المشهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest degree of predominant opinion called?
A) Al-yaqin
B) Al-mashhūr
C) Al-shakk
D) Al-wahm

Key Takeaway
Predominant opinion (al-żann al-rājiḥ) spans from near-certainty (al-mashhūr) down to the edge of doubt, with multiple intermediate degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following is an example of the highest degree of predominant opinion (al-mashhūr) in Islamic ethics: 'Justice is obligatory' or 'Charity is recommended'. Explain your reasoning using the author's criterion.

Doubt and Weak Opinion
Definition of doubt and weak opinion with balance analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish doubt (al-shakk) from weak opinion (al-żann al-marjūḥ) and explain their relationship as opposite ends of the balance scale.

Core Idea
Doubt is perfect equipoise; weak opinion is the inverse of predominant opinion.

Below predominant opinion lies doubt, where probabilities are exactly equal. Further down is weak opinion (also called 'illusion'), which is the mirror image of predominant opinion: as one side becomes more likely, the other becomes weaker. The author uses the image of a balance scale to illustrate this inverse relationship.

Guided Path
1
Define doubt as perfect equality
Find the sentence: 'مرتبة تتساوى فيها الاحتمالات تساوياً تاماً'. This defines doubt as complete equality, no preponderance.
2
Contrast doubt with predominant opinion
In predominant opinion there is preponderance; in doubt there is none. So doubt is the boundary where preponderance disappears.
3
Define weak opinion as the opposite
The author calls it 'الظن المرجوح' and says it is 'الظن الوهمي المقابل للظن الراجح'. It is the inverse; as predominant opinion increases, weak opinion decreases proportionally.
4
Understand the balance scale analogy
The author says 'كأنهما في كفتي ميزان'. If one side gains weight (preponderance), the other becomes lighter. This helps visualise the inverse relationship.

The Full Spectrum: From Certainty to Certain Falsehood
Completing the hierarchy with certain falsehood and diagrams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the complete hierarchy of certainty from certain truth down to certain falsehood, and explain how the circle closes.

Core Idea
Certain falsehood is the mirror of certain truth, completing the epistemological circle.

The author completes the hierarchy by placing certain falsehood (al-bāṭil bi-yaqīn) below weak opinion. This level is also a form of certainty – negative certainty. The circle closes because the lowest meets the highest in certainty. The diagrams illustrate how weak opinion approaches the 'ceiling of frivolity' while its opposite rests on the 'floor of preponderance'.

Guided Path
1
Locate the level below weak opinion
Find the sentence: 'وليس دون مرتبة الظن المرجوح إلا مرتبة الباطل بيقين'. That is the only level below weak opinion.
2
Understand how it enters into generality of certainty
The author says 'وعندئذ يدخل في عموم اليقين'. Even though it is false, it is certain falsehood – a form of negative certainty.
3
Interpret the 'ceiling of frivolity' and 'floor of preponderance'
The ceiling is the upper bound of weak opinion (i.e., certain falsehood); the floor is the lower bound of predominant opinion (i.e., certain truth). The extremes are certainties.
4
Visualise using the diagrams described
The first figure has a vertical scale from floor to ceiling; the second figure shows a horizontal balance with certain falsehood on one end and certain truth on the other.
The diagrams are not text, but the description suffices.

Weak opinion: And below the rank of doubt comes the rank of weak opinion, and it is the illusory opinion opposite to predominant opinion, and therefore it is called (illusion), and it has degrees corresponding to the degrees of predominant opinion. For as much as the preponderance of the opposite probability, so is its weakness; if the opposite probability is close to certainty of affirmation, then it is close to certainty of negation, and if it is below that descending towards the rank of doubt, then it ascends towards the rank of doubt as well until they approximate, as if they are on two pans of a balance scale; whenever one of them increases in preponderance, its opposite increases in lightness and weakness, and whenever one of them decreases in preponderance, its opposite increases in its approximation to it. The rank below weak opinion: There is nothing below the rank of weak opinion except the rank of certain falsehood, and then it enters into the generality of certainty, and the circle closes. And when weak opinion reaches the rank of certain falsehood, it hits the ceiling of frivolity, just as its opposite hits the floor of certain truth, and the bar chart represents it in the following first figure, or the horizontal of the scale becomes vertical, and the bar chart represents it in the following second figure: First figure Ceiling of frivolity and to it ends certain falsehood Degrees of weak opinion "illusion" Doubt Doubt Degrees of predominant opinion Floor of preponderance and at it rests certain truth Second figure Doubt Doubt Predominant opinion Weak opinion " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " " Certain falsehood Certain truth Ways to attain knowledge: The process of seeking knowledge is a process in which cooperate the external and internal senses, the instruments and tools that the senses use, and the innate scales of the intellect.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage continues a classification of degrees of certainty, focusing on levels below the highest certainty. The author begins by referring to two previously mentioned levels ('above them two') — likely the highest degrees of certainty or immediate knowledge — and describes a state that gives complete tranquility to the heart and soul, enabling one to control one's behavior. Then the author defines the lower levels in order: 1) Predominant opinion (al-żann al-rājiḥ): this rank is below certainty but still close; it is divided into degrees, the highest being 'al-mashhūr' (well-known), which is so close to certainty that its opposite is hardly conceivable (e.g., most judgments about justice, virtues, vices). The degrees descend until the lowest degree is adjacent to doubt. 2) Doubt (al-shakk): below predominant opinion, where probabilities are exactly equal, so no side outweighs the other. Perception here has no preponderance. 3) Weak opinion (al-żann al-marjūḥ): below doubt, it is the opposite of predominant opinion, also called 'illusion' (wahm). It has degrees that correspond inversely to the degrees of predominant opinion: as the opposite probability increases, this weakens proportionally. The author uses the analogy of a balance scale: as one side becomes heavier, the other becomes lighter. 4) Below weak opinion is only certain falsehood (al-bāṭil bi-yaqīn), which then falls under 'certainty' in a broad sense, completing the circle. At the extreme, weak opinion hits the 'ceiling of frivolity' while its opposite (certain truth) rests on the 'floor of preponderance'. The author mentions two diagrams (shown in the text) to illustrate this. Finally, the author transitions to a new section on the ways to attain knowledge, emphasizing that it involves cooperation of external and internal senses, instruments, and the innate faculties of the intellect. The passage is primarily descriptive and classificatory, aiming to map the epistemological spectrum from certainty to falsehood.

Key Terms
الباطل بيقين
سقف الطيش
أرضية الحق بيقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only level below weak opinion?
A) Predominant opinion
B) Doubt
C) Certain falsehood
D) Certain truth

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy closes with certain falsehood, which is a form of certainty (negative), completing the symmetrical spectrum from certain truth to certain falsehood with doubt at the centre.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following statements using the hierarchy: (a) 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) said X' when the hadith is sahih; (b) 'The Prophet said Y' when the hadith is da'if; (c) 'The Prophet said Z' when the hadith is fabricated. Which level does each belong to?

The Role of Senses and Innate Laws
Author explains sources of knowledge: external/internal senses, intellect's innate laws, and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of senses (external and internal) and the concept of innate rational laws as sources of knowledge according to the author.

Core Idea
Knowledge begins with the senses but is judged by the intellect's innate laws.

This passage lays the foundation for Islamic epistemology by describing how humans acquire knowledge. The author distinguishes between external and internal senses, and then introduces the innate rational laws that the intellect brings to bear on sensory data. Understanding these sources is essential for evaluating how Islamic scholars build arguments from observation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources of knowledge
Read the first two paragraphs of the Arabic. The author mentions two primary means: experiences through external and internal senses. Note that prophets have a special distinction in inner senses.
2
Distinguish external from internal senses
Internal senses (الحواس الباطنة) process and refine sensory data; they vary among people. The author does not list them individually but highlights their importance.
3
Define innate rational laws
The intellect adds 'basic scales' (قوانين الفكر العقلية) to sensory materials. These are innate principles that allow the intellect to judge ideas by affirmation or negation.
4
Apply to Islamic theology
Consider how this framework is used in kalam: sensory observation of the world's temporality combined with the innate law of causality leads to the proof of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الباطنة قوةً وضعفاً، وبعض الحواس الباطنة يمتاز بها بعض الموهوبين امتيازاً فائقاً، وللأنبياء نصيب من امتياز الحواس الباطنة لا يوجد عند غيرهم.
وهكذا تغدو الخبرات التي يمارسها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة والباطنة أولى وسائل اكتسابه للمعارف.
جـ - والفكر يمارس أعماله بعد أن توجد لديه مجموعة من مواد المعرفة، التي وردت إليه عن طريق الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، ويضيف إليها موازين أساسية موجودة لديه في أصل فطرته، قد يطلق عليها قوانين الفكر العقلية، وبهذه الموازين الفطرية يستطيع العقل أن يحكم على كثير من الأفكار بالإثبات أو بالنفي.
وبهذه الموازين الفطرية العقلية يحكم العقل أحكاماً ذاتية فيما يلي:
١ - إنه يحكم بالتوحيد بين الفكرة وماهيتها المكونة لها، وهو المعبر عنه، بما هو هو.
٢ - إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض أو ما يؤدي إلى التناقض، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون عدم التناقض). ككون الشيء موجوداً ومعدوماً في آن واحد ومكان واحد، وكون جزء الشيء أكبر من كله، مع محافظة الجزء على كونه جزءاً، ومحافظة الكل على كونه كلاً لهذا الجزء.
٣ - ويحكم العقل بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون الامتناع) أو (قانون الوسط المرفوع) إذ ليس بين الوجود والعدم وسط يعتبر شيئاً ثالثاً غير الوجود والعدم، وهذا هو ما يعبر عنه باستحالة رفع النقيضين.
٤ - ويحكم العقل بواحد من أحكامه الثلاثة التي يحكم بها على كل ما يقع في تصوره، وهي: (واجب الوجود فلا يُتصوّر في العقل عدمه - واجب العدم أو مستحيل الوجود فلا يتصور في العقل وجوده ـ ممكن الوجود ويتوقف وجوده على علة أو سبب).
ه - ويحكم العقل بأن كل حادث لم يكن ثم كان، لا بد له من سبب غيره سابق عليه في الوجود اقتضى وجوده.
٦ - ويحكم العقل بقابلية العدد للتسلسل إلا ما لا نهاية له في التصور.
٧ - ويحكم العقل باستحالة الدور وهو توقف وجود الشيء على وجوده نفسه، وباستحالة تسلسل الحوادث إلى ما لا نهاية له في الوجود الأول (الماضي).
٨ - وقدرة التخيل لدى العقل تستطيع تصور مركبات جديدة غير موجودة في الواقع بهيئتها التركيبية، ولكنها موجودة بأجزائها، وهذه القدرة تنتزعها من أماكنها وتؤلف بينها في صورة مبتكرة.
وقدرة التخيل مهما كانت واسعة المدى في الإبداع والابتكار فإنها لا تستطيع أن تبتدع جزءاً لم ترد إليها صورته عن طريق الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، وتنحصر إبداعاتها وابتكاراتها في تخيل تركيبات جديدة على غير مثال سبق، عن طريق التحليل والتركيب، والجمع والتفريق، وإضافة مقادير إلى ما لا نهاية له، ونقص مقادير حتى آخر جزء ممكن.
حدود العقل:
وللعقل ـ كما للحس الظاهر والحس الباطن ـ حدود ينتهي إليها، متى وصل إليها أعلن عجزه.
إنه محدود بين شيئين هما الزمان والمكان، لذلك يسأل دائماً متى؟ وأين؟ مع أن الحقائق الكبرى لا مكان لها ولا زمان، ولكن العقل عاجز عن تصور ذلك.
إنه محدود حينما يعلن عجزه عن التسليم بواحد من احتمالين لا ثالث لهما، هما هل الكون متناهي الحدود؟ أو هو غير متناهي الحدود؟.
People vary in the levels of their inner senses in strength and weakness, and some inner senses are distinguished by some gifted individuals with superior distinction, and prophets have a share of distinction in inner senses that is not found in others. Thus, the experiences that a person exercises through his external and internal senses become the primary means of his acquisition of knowledge. C - The intellect exercises its work after a collection of materials of knowledge exists for it, which came to it through the external and internal senses, and it adds to them basic scales present in it in the original disposition of its nature, which may be called the rational laws of thought, and with these innate scales the intellect can judge many ideas by affirmation or negation. And with these innate rational scales, the intellect makes self-evident judgments regarding the following: 1 - It judges by the unification between an idea and its constituting essence, which is expressed as 'what it is'. 2 - It judges the impossibility of contradiction or what leads to contradiction, and this goes back to the rational principle called the 'Law of Non-Contradiction', such as a thing being existent and non-existent at the same time and place, and a part of a thing being greater than its whole, while preserving the part being a part and the whole being a whole for this part. 3 - And the intellect judges that a thing is either existent or non-existent, and this goes back to the rational principle called the 'Law of Prevention' or the 'Law of Excluded Middle', for there is no middle between existence and non-existence that is considered a third thing other than existence and non-existence, and this is what is expressed as the impossibility of both negations. 4 - And the intellect judges by one of its three judgments that it makes on everything that falls into its conception, which are: necessary existence (its non-existence cannot be conceived in the mind), necessary non-existence or impossible existence (its existence cannot be conceived in the mind), possible existence (its existence depends on a cause or reason). 5 - And the intellect judges that every originated thing that was not then was, must have a cause other than it prior to it in existence that necessitated its existence. 6 - And the intellect judges the susceptibility of number to infinity except what has no end in conception. 7 - And the intellect judges the impossibility of circularity, which is the dependence of the existence of a thing on its own existence, and the impossibility of an infinite regress of events in the first (past) existence. 8 - And the imaginative power of the intellect can conceive new composites not existing in reality in their compositional form, but they exist in their parts, and this power extracts them from their places and combines them in an innovative form. And the imaginative power, however vast its scope in creativity and innovation, cannot invent a part whose image has not come to it through external or internal sense, and its creations and innovations are limited to imagining new compositions without a previous model, through analysis and synthesis, combination and separation, addition of quantities to infinity, and subtraction of quantities to the last possible part. Limits of the intellect: And the intellect, like the external sense and the internal sense, has limits it reaches; when it reaches them it declares its inability. It is limited between two things: time and space, so it constantly asks 'when?' and 'where?', even though the great truths have no place or time, but the intellect is incapable of conceiving that. It is limited when it declares its inability to accept one of two possibilities with no third: is the universe finite in limits or is it infinite in limits?.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition by the author describing the sources and faculties of human knowledge, followed by the intellect's innate principles and its limitations. The author begins by noting individual differences in internal senses, with prophets having a special distinction. Then he states that experiences from external and internal senses are the primary means of acquiring knowledge. The intellect then works on these materials using innate rational scales or laws. The author lists eight self-evident judgments of the intellect: (1) the principle of identity (a thing is what it is), (2) the law of non-contradiction (a thing cannot both be and not be at the same time in the same respect), (3) the law of excluded middle (no middle between existence and non-existence), (4) the three modal judgments (necessary, impossible, possible), (5) the principle of causality (every event has a cause), (6) the concept of mathematical infinity, (7) the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and (8) the nature of imagination as composing new forms from existing parts. After that, the author discusses the limits of the intellect: it is bound by time and space, and cannot conclusively answer whether the universe is finite or infinite. Throughout, the author uses no formal logical arguments but rather expounds on the foundational epistemic principles in a clear, enumerative style. All examples are from general philosophy; the commentary here preserves that style without adding external content.

Key Terms
الحواس الباطنة
قوانين الفكر العقلية

A) The intellect alone without senses
B) Experiences through external and internal senses
C) Prophetic revelation exclusively
D) Innate rational laws without sensory input

Key Takeaway
Knowledge acquisition begins with sensory experience, but the intellect applies innate rational laws to judge and organize that experience.

Laws of Non-Contradiction and Excluded Middle
Author explains sources of knowledge: external/internal senses, intellect's innate laws, and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle as presented in the passage, and provide an Islamic theological example of each.

Core Idea
The intellect cannot deny that a thing either is or is not; these are its founding principles.

Among the self-evident judgments of the intellect, the author highlights two fundamental laws: non-contradiction and excluded middle. These are the bedrock of rational thought in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. This lesson focuses on understanding these two laws and their application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the two laws in the passage
Locate the sentences beginning with '٢ - إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض' and '٣ - ويحكم العقل بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً'.
2
Paraphrase each law
Non-contradiction: A thing cannot be both existent and non-existent at the same time in the same respect. Excluded middle: There is no third alternative between existence and non-existence.
3
Give an Islamic example
For non-contradiction: In kalam, God cannot be both eternal and temporal; that would be contradictory. For excluded middle: A ruling in fiqh is either obligatory or not obligatory; no middle state exists.
4
Distinguish the two laws
Non-contradiction says you cannot affirm both a proposition and its negation. Excluded middle says you cannot deny both; one of them must be true.

The author lists eight self-evident judgments of the intellect: (1) the principle of identity (a thing is what it is), (2) the law of non-contradiction (a thing cannot both be and not be at the same time in the same respect), (3) the law of excluded middle (no middle between existence and non-existence), (4) the three modal judgments (necessary, impossible, possible), (5) the principle of causality (every event has a cause), (6) the concept of mathematical infinity, (7) the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and (8) the nature of imagination as composing new forms from existing parts. After that, the author discusses the limits of the intellect: it is bound by time and space, and cannot conclusively answer whether the universe is finite or infinite. Throughout, the author uses no formal logical arguments but rather expounds on the foundational epistemic principles in a clear, enumerative style. All examples are from general philosophy; the commentary here preserves that style without adding external content.

The Limits of Reason: Time, Space, and the Infinite
Author explains sources of knowledge: external/internal senses, intellect's innate laws, and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three limitations of the intellect as described in the passage and relate them to debates in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Reason reaches its boundary when confronted with the timeless and the infinite.

The author concludes the passage by discussing the limits of the intellect. Like the senses, reason has boundaries: it is bound by time and space, and it cannot decisively answer whether the universe is finite or infinite. This lesson explores these limitations and their implications for Islamic theological discussions, such as the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Locate the section on limits
Find the paragraph beginning 'حدود العقل:' and read until the end.
2
List the two boundaries
The author says the intellect is limited between time and space. It constantly asks 'when?' and 'where?' but great truths have no place or time.
3
Identify the specific paradox
The intellect cannot decide whether the universe is finite or infinite; both possibilities are conceivable but neither can be proven by reason alone.
4
Connect to Islamic theology
In kalam, this limitation is used by theologians who argue that the world must be temporally originated (hadith) because an infinite regress is impossible. The author's point shows that reason cannot settle this on its own, leaving room for revelation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الباطنة قوةً وضعفاً، وبعض الحواس الباطنة يمتاز بها بعض الموهوبين امتيازاً فائقاً، وللأنبياء نصيب من امتياز الحواس الباطنة لا يوجد عند غيرهم.
وهكذا تغدو الخبرات التي يمارسها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة والباطنة أولى وسائل اكتسابه للمعارف.
جـ - والفكر يمارس أعماله بعد أن توجد لديه مجموعة من مواد المعرفة، التي وردت إليه عن طريق الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، ويضيف إليها موازين أساسية موجودة لديه في أصل فطرته، قد يطلق عليها قوانين الفكر العقلية، وبهذه الموازين الفطرية يستطيع العقل أن يحكم على كثير من الأفكار بالإثبات أو بالنفي.
وبهذه الموازين الفطرية العقلية يحكم العقل أحكاماً ذاتية فيما يلي:
١ - إنه يحكم بالتوحيد بين الفكرة وماهيتها المكونة لها، وهو المعبر عنه، بما هو هو.
٢ - إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض أو ما يؤدي إلى التناقض، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون عدم التناقض). ككون الشيء موجوداً ومعدوماً في آن واحد ومكان واحد، وكون جزء الشيء أكبر من كله، مع محافظة الجزء على كونه جزءاً، ومحافظة الكل على كونه كلاً لهذا الجزء.
٣ - ويحكم العقل بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون الامتناع) أو (قانون الوسط المرفوع) إذ ليس بين الوجود والعدم وسط يعتبر شيئاً ثالثاً غير الوجود والعدم، وهذا هو ما يعبر عنه باستحالة رفع النقيضين.
٤ - ويحكم العقل بواحد من أحكامه الثلاثة التي يحكم بها على كل ما يقع في تصوره، وهي: (واجب الوجود فلا يُتصوّر في العقل عدمه - واجب العدم أو مستحيل الوجود فلا يتصور في العقل وجوده ـ ممكن الوجود ويتوقف وجوده على علة أو سبب).
ه - ويحكم العقل بأن كل حادث لم يكن ثم كان، لا بد له من سبب غيره سابق عليه في الوجود اقتضى وجوده.
٦ - ويحكم العقل بقابلية العدد للتسلسل إلا ما لا نهاية له في التصور.
٧ - ويحكم العقل باستحالة الدور وهو توقف وجود الشيء على وجوده نفسه، وباستحالة تسلسل الحوادث إلى ما لا نهاية له في الوجود الأول (الماضي).
٨ - وقدرة التخيل لدى العقل تستطيع تصور مركبات جديدة غير موجودة في الواقع بهيئتها التركيبية، ولكنها موجودة بأجزائها، وهذه القدرة تنتزعها من أماكنها وتؤلف بينها في صورة مبتكرة.
وقدرة التخيل مهما كانت واسعة المدى في الإبداع والابتكار فإنها لا تستطيع أن تبتدع جزءاً لم ترد إليها صورته عن طريق الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، وتنحصر إبداعاتها وابتكاراتها في تخيل تركيبات جديدة على غير مثال سبق، عن طريق التحليل والتركيب، والجمع والتفريق، وإضافة مقادير إلى ما لا نهاية له، ونقص مقادير حتى آخر جزء ممكن.
حدود العقل:
وللعقل ـ كما للحس الظاهر والحس الباطن ـ حدود ينتهي إليها، متى وصل إليها أعلن عجزه.
إنه محدود بين شيئين هما الزمان والمكان، لذلك يسأل دائماً متى؟ وأين؟ مع أن الحقائق الكبرى لا مكان لها ولا زمان، ولكن العقل عاجز عن تصور ذلك.
إنه محدود حينما يعلن عجزه عن التسليم بواحد من احتمالين لا ثالث لهما، هما هل الكون متناهي الحدود؟ أو هو غير متناهي الحدود؟.
People vary in the levels of their inner senses in strength and weakness, and some inner senses are distinguished by some gifted individuals with superior distinction, and prophets have a share of distinction in inner senses that is not found in others. Thus, the experiences that a person exercises through his external and internal senses become the primary means of his acquisition of knowledge. C - The intellect exercises its work after a collection of materials of knowledge exists for it, which came to it through the external and internal senses, and it adds to them basic scales present in it in the original disposition of its nature, which may be called the rational laws of thought, and with these innate scales the intellect can judge many ideas by affirmation or negation. And with these innate rational scales, the intellect makes self-evident judgments regarding the following: 1 - It judges by the unification between an idea and its constituting essence, which is expressed as 'what it is'. 2 - It judges the impossibility of contradiction or what leads to contradiction, and this goes back to the rational principle called the 'Law of Non-Contradiction', such as a thing being existent and non-existent at the same time and place, and a part of a thing being greater than its whole, while preserving the part being a part and the whole being a whole for this part. 3 - And the intellect judges that a thing is either existent or non-existent, and this goes back to the rational principle called the 'Law of Prevention' or the 'Law of Excluded Middle', for there is no middle between existence and non-existence that is considered a third thing other than existence and non-existence, and this is what is expressed as the impossibility of both negations. 4 - And the intellect judges by one of its three judgments that it makes on everything that falls into its conception, which are: necessary existence (its non-existence cannot be conceived in the mind), necessary non-existence or impossible existence (its existence cannot be conceived in the mind), possible existence (its existence depends on a cause or reason). 5 - And the intellect judges that every originated thing that was not then was, must have a cause other than it prior to it in existence that necessitated its existence. 6 - And the intellect judges the susceptibility of number to infinity except what has no end in conception. 7 - And the intellect judges the impossibility of circularity, which is the dependence of the existence of a thing on its own existence, and the impossibility of an infinite regress of events in the first (past) existence. 8 - And the imaginative power of the intellect can conceive new composites not existing in reality in their compositional form, but they exist in their parts, and this power extracts them from their places and combines them in an innovative form. And the imaginative power, however vast its scope in creativity and innovation, cannot invent a part whose image has not come to it through external or internal sense, and its creations and innovations are limited to imagining new compositions without a previous model, through analysis and synthesis, combination and separation, addition of quantities to infinity, and subtraction of quantities to the last possible part. Limits of the intellect: And the intellect, like the external sense and the internal sense, has limits it reaches; when it reaches them it declares its inability. It is limited between two things: time and space, so it constantly asks 'when?' and 'where?', even though the great truths have no place or time, but the intellect is incapable of conceiving that. It is limited when it declares its inability to accept one of two possibilities with no third: is the universe finite in limits or is it infinite in limits?.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition by the author describing the sources and faculties of human knowledge, followed by the intellect's innate principles and its limitations. The author begins by noting individual differences in internal senses, with prophets having a special distinction. Then he states that experiences from external and internal senses are the primary means of acquiring knowledge. The intellect then works on these materials using innate rational scales or laws. The author lists eight self-evident judgments of the intellect: (1) the principle of identity (a thing is what it is), (2) the law of non-contradiction (a thing cannot both be and not be at the same time in the same respect), (3) the law of excluded middle (no middle between existence and non-existence), (4) the three modal judgments (necessary, impossible, possible), (5) the principle of causality (every event has a cause), (6) the concept of mathematical infinity, (7) the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and (8) the nature of imagination as composing new forms from existing parts. After that, the author discusses the limits of the intellect: it is bound by time and space, and cannot conclusively answer whether the universe is finite or infinite. Throughout, the author uses no formal logical arguments but rather expounds on the foundational epistemic principles in a clear, enumerative style. All examples are from general philosophy; the commentary here preserves that style without adding external content.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Six Foundational Intellectual Operations (1-6)
First six intellectual operations listed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the first six intellectual operations (deduction, analogy, contraposition, analysis/synthesis, relation perception, and causal reasoning) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The intellect's basic toolkit: deduction, analogy, and analysis underlie all Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

The first six operations form the core of logical reasoning: deducing particulars from universals, analogizing similar cases, perceiving opposites, analyzing and synthesizing, grasping relations, and tracing causes. These are the building blocks of qiyas, istidlal, and tahlil in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first operation: deduction and induction
Find where the passage says the intellect deduces particulars from universals and generalizes through inherent laws or induction. Note that this covers both deductive and inductive reasoning.
Deduction (istinbāṭ) is central to deriving rulings from general principles.
2
Recognize the second operation: analogy (qiyas)
Locate the phrase 'قياس الأشباه والنظائر' (analogizing similar things). This is the qiyas used in fiqh to extend rulings.
Analogy is a key tool for mujtahids when no direct text exists.
3
Distinguish the third operation: opposites and converses
The passage explains that knowing a concept lets the intellect perceive its opposite (naqīḍ) and its converse ('aks). This underpins logical transformations like contraposition.
In logic, the converse of 'every A is B' is 'some B is A'.
4
Analyze the fourth operation: analysis, synthesis, combination, separation
The intellect can break down perceptions (analysis) and combine them (synthesis). This is essential for tafsīr (analysis of verses) and uṣūl (synthesis of principles).
Al-Taḥlīl wa al-tarkīb are terms used in Arabic grammar and rhetoric.
5
Explore the fifth operation: perception of relations
The intellect perceives relations (nisāb) between meanings and perceptions. In Kalam, this includes relations like causality, priority, and similarity.
Relation perception is critical for understanding the argument from causation.

أعمال العقل الاستنباطية والاستدلالية :
وبما يجتمع لدى العقل من صور المدركات عن طريق الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، أو عن طريق أخبار الآخرين التي تحكي ما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وبما يتخيله من صور تركيبية جديدة يبتكرها، وبما لديه من أصول فطرية تتألف منها موازينه وقوانينه الذاتية، يستطيع أن يقوم بأعمال فكرية كثيرة، منها الأعمال التالية :
١ - يستطيع العقل بنفسه استنباط الجزئيات من الكليات، وإدراك الكليات من النظر في الجزئيات، وتعميم الأحكام عن طريق قوانينه الذاتية، أو عن طريق الاستقراء .
٢ - ويستطيع العقل قياس الأشباه والنظائر بعضها على بعض .
٣ - ويستطيع العقل استنباط مقابلات المعاني وعكوسها، فيدرك النقيض وما هو داخل فيه متى عرف النقيض، ويدرك العكس متى عرف الأصل .
٤ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يجري أعمال التحليل والتركيب والجمع والتفريق فيما لديه من مدركات .
٥ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يدرك النسب بين المعاني والمدركات التي لديه .
٦ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه إدراك الروابط بين المعلولات وعللها العقلية، وبين المسببات وأسبابها المنطقية، وأن يدرك آثار الأشياء ونتائجها المنطقية المستندة إلى مبدأ العلة العقلية، أو السبب المنطقي .
٧ - ويستطيع العقل أن يدرك من ملاحظته للتجارب المتكررة أحكاماً عامة، ولو لم يكن بين السبب والظاهرة ارتباط عقلي .
٨ - ومتى عرف العقل الشيء الناقص، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق في سُلَّم كمال هذا الشيء متعرفاً على درجاته درجة فدرجة، ضمن قوانين التكامل.
٩ - ومتى أدرك العقل الشيء الكامل أو المرتقي في درجات الكمال، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق فيتصور احتمالات نقصه دركة فدركة حتى أسفلها .
١٠ - ومتى أدرك العقل الجميل وأحسّ بمواطن جماله استطاع أن ينطلق بنفسه فيتصور الاحتمالات التي تشوّهه وتقبّحه فتجعله غير جميل .
١١ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات المقادير فيزيد فيها وينقص إلى ما لا نهاية له ؛ فيزيد الكميات وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد المكانية وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد الزمانية وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الحرارة وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الضغط وينقصها، وهكذا إلى كل شيء خاضع لحساب المقادير .
١٢ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات اختلاف الكيفيات إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيغير فيها ويبدّل بشكل لا حصر له، فلو أمسك عجينة من الشمع المطاوع، وانطلق يطرح احتمالات تغيير كيفيتها لما وقفت تغييراته عند حد .
وهكذا يتضح لنا كيف يقوم العقل بأعماله التي لا حصر لها، ضمن المواد الأولى التي تصل إليه عن طريق الحسّ الظاهر أو الباطن، أو التي تصل إليه عن طريق أخبار الآخرين وشهاداتهم، مضافاً إليها ما لديه من قوانين ذاتية، وما لديه من قدرة على التخيل .
ولكن ينبغي أن نعلم أنه ليس كل ما يدركه الفكر هو من قبيل اليقين، بل هو ينقسم إلى أقسام الإدراك التي سبق بيانها : [الحق بيقين - الظن الراجح - الشك - الظن المرجوح (الوهم) - الباطل بيقين].
وعلى العقل أن يصنف معارفه، ويضع كلاً منها في مرتبته ودرجته التي
The Deductive and Inferential Works of the Intellect:
With what gathers in the intellect from forms of perceptions through the external or internal sense, or through reports of others that relate what they have arrived at of knowledge, and with what it imagines of new composite forms it invents, and with what it has of innate principles from which its measures and its inherent laws are composed, it can perform many intellectual works, among them the following works:
1 - The intellect can by itself deduce particulars from universals, and perceive universals from examining particulars, and generalize rulings through its inherent laws, or through induction.
2 - And the intellect can analogize similar things and parallels to one another.
3 - And the intellect can deduce opposites of meanings and their converses, so it perceives the contradictory and what is contained within it when it knows the contradictory, and it perceives the converse when it knows the original.
4 - And the intellect can by itself perform operations of analysis, synthesis, combination, and separation on the perceptions it has.
5 - And the intellect can by itself perceive the relations between meanings and perceptions that it has.
6 - And the intellect can by itself perceive the connections between effects and their intellectual causes, and between caused things and their logical reasons, and it can perceive the traces of things and their logical results that rely on the principle of intellectual causality, or logical reason.
7 - And the intellect can perceive from its observation of repeated experiments general rulings, even if there is no intellectual connection between the cause and the phenomenon.
8 - And when the intellect knows an imperfect thing, it can by itself ascend the ladder of perfection of that thing, recognizing its degrees degree by degree, within the laws of completion.
9 - And when the intellect perceives a perfect thing or one advanced in degrees of perfection, it can by itself descend to imagine possibilities of its deficiency degree by degree down to its lowest.
10 - And when the intellect perceives the beautiful and feels the loci of its beauty, it can by itself imagine possibilities that disfigure it and make it ugly, rendering it not beautiful.
11 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in perceiving possibilities of quantities, increasing them and decreasing them without end; increasing and decreasing quantities, increasing and decreasing spatial dimensions, increasing and decreasing temporal dimensions, increasing and decreasing measures of heat, increasing and decreasing measures of pressure, and so on for everything subject to measurement.
12 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in perceiving possibilities of variation of qualities without end, changing them and altering them without limit. If it takes a lump of malleable wax and proceeds to propose possibilities of changing its quality, its changes would not stop at a limit.
Thus it becomes clear to us how the intellect performs its endless works, within the primary materials that reach it through the external or internal sense, or that reach it through reports of others and their testimonies, added to what it has of inherent laws and what it has of imaginative ability.
But we should know that not everything the intellect perceives is of the category of certainty; rather it is divided into the divisions of perception previously explained: [truth with certainty - preponderant opinion - doubt - weak opinion (illusion) - falsehood with certainty].
And the intellect must classify its knowledge, and place each of them in its rank and degree.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological enumeration of the intellect's deductive and inferential operations. The author lists twelve abilities, each building on the previous or illustrating a different aspect of mental processing. The first ability is deduction from universals to particulars and induction from particulars to universals, highlighting the mind's capacity for generalization. The second is analogical reasoning (qiyas) among similar things. The third explains perception of opposites and converses, fundamental in logic. The fourth through sixth involve analysis, synthesis, relation perception, and causal connection. The seventh allows for generalizations from repeated experiments even without a rational causal link, indicating empirical reasoning. The eighth and ninth show the mind's ability to traverse scales from imperfection to perfection and vice versa. The tenth deals with aesthetic judgment. The eleventh and twelfth involve infinite variation of quantities and qualities, exemplified by wax. The passage concludes by acknowledging that not all intellectual products are certain; they fall into categories from certainty to falsehood, and the intellect must classify accordingly. This lesson is a structured overview of the mind's capacities, foundational for understanding the scope of logic and epistemology in Islamic thought.

Six Advanced Intellectual Operations (7-12)
Last six operations and epistemic classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the last six intellectual operations (empirical generalization, perfection ascent/descent, aesthetic variation, quantitative and qualitative infinite variation) and classify knowledge according to the five epistemic categories.

Core Idea
Beyond deduction, the intellect generalizes from experience, scales perfection, imagines variations, and classifies knowledge by certainty.

The last six operations extend the intellect's reach: it can derive general rules from repeated experiments, move along scales of perfection, imagine aesthetic and quantitative changes ad infinitum, and finally classify all knowledge into degrees of certainty. These operations connect logic to empirical science, aesthetics, and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Discover operation 7: empirical generalization without rational link
The passage says the intellect can form general rulings from repeated experiments even without a necessary intellectual connection between cause and effect. This is inductive generalization.
Example: Knowing that fire burns by repeated observation, even if we don't grasp the rational necessity.
2
Trace operations 8 and 9: the perfection ladder
When knowing an imperfect thing, the intellect ascends the ladder of perfection degree by degree. When knowing a perfect thing, it descends to imagine deficiency levels.
This is used in kalam to argue from the imperfection of the world to a perfect Creator.
3
Understand operation 10: aesthetic variation
Perceiving beauty, the intellect can imagine ways to disfigure or uglify it. This shows the intellect's creative, aesthetic judgment.
Relevant to evaluating the eloquence of Quranic verses or beauty in nature.
4
Comprehend operation 11: infinite quantitative variation
The intellect can infinitely increase or decrease quantities: sizes, dimensions, heat, pressure, etc. This underlies mathematical reasoning.
Example: Dividing inheritance shares (fara'id) uses quantitative operations.
5
Comprehend operation 12: infinite qualitative variation
The intellect can imagine endless variations in qualities, like changing the shape of wax. This shows creative imagination.
Used in arguments about the infinite possibilities of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعمال العقل الاستنباطية والاستدلالية :
وبما يجتمع لدى العقل من صور المدركات عن طريق الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، أو عن طريق أخبار الآخرين التي تحكي ما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وبما يتخيله من صور تركيبية جديدة يبتكرها، وبما لديه من أصول فطرية تتألف منها موازينه وقوانينه الذاتية، يستطيع أن يقوم بأعمال فكرية كثيرة، منها الأعمال التالية :
١ - يستطيع العقل بنفسه استنباط الجزئيات من الكليات، وإدراك الكليات من النظر في الجزئيات، وتعميم الأحكام عن طريق قوانينه الذاتية، أو عن طريق الاستقراء .
٢ - ويستطيع العقل قياس الأشباه والنظائر بعضها على بعض .
٣ - ويستطيع العقل استنباط مقابلات المعاني وعكوسها، فيدرك النقيض وما هو داخل فيه متى عرف النقيض، ويدرك العكس متى عرف الأصل .
٤ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يجري أعمال التحليل والتركيب والجمع والتفريق فيما لديه من مدركات .
٥ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يدرك النسب بين المعاني والمدركات التي لديه .
٦ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه إدراك الروابط بين المعلولات وعللها العقلية، وبين المسببات وأسبابها المنطقية، وأن يدرك آثار الأشياء ونتائجها المنطقية المستندة إلى مبدأ العلة العقلية، أو السبب المنطقي .
٧ - ويستطيع العقل أن يدرك من ملاحظته للتجارب المتكررة أحكاماً عامة، ولو لم يكن بين السبب والظاهرة ارتباط عقلي .
٨ - ومتى عرف العقل الشيء الناقص، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق في سُلَّم كمال هذا الشيء متعرفاً على درجاته درجة فدرجة، ضمن قوانين التكامل.
٩ - ومتى أدرك العقل الشيء الكامل أو المرتقي في درجات الكمال، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق فيتصور احتمالات نقصه دركة فدركة حتى أسفلها .
١٠ - ومتى أدرك العقل الجميل وأحسّ بمواطن جماله استطاع أن ينطلق بنفسه فيتصور الاحتمالات التي تشوّهه وتقبّحه فتجعله غير جميل .
١١ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات المقادير فيزيد فيها وينقص إلى ما لا نهاية له ؛ فيزيد الكميات وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد المكانية وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد الزمانية وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الحرارة وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الضغط وينقصها، وهكذا إلى كل شيء خاضع لحساب المقادير .
١٢ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات اختلاف الكيفيات إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيغير فيها ويبدّل بشكل لا حصر له، فلو أمسك عجينة من الشمع المطاوع، وانطلق يطرح احتمالات تغيير كيفيتها لما وقفت تغييراته عند حد .
وهكذا يتضح لنا كيف يقوم العقل بأعماله التي لا حصر لها، ضمن المواد الأولى التي تصل إليه عن طريق الحسّ الظاهر أو الباطن، أو التي تصل إليه عن طريق أخبار الآخرين وشهاداتهم، مضافاً إليها ما لديه من قوانين ذاتية، وما لديه من قدرة على التخيل .
ولكن ينبغي أن نعلم أنه ليس كل ما يدركه الفكر هو من قبيل اليقين، بل هو ينقسم إلى أقسام الإدراك التي سبق بيانها : [الحق بيقين - الظن الراجح - الشك - الظن المرجوح (الوهم) - الباطل بيقين].
وعلى العقل أن يصنف معارفه، ويضع كلاً منها في مرتبته ودرجته التي
The Deductive and Inferential Works of the Intellect:
With what gathers in the intellect from forms of perceptions through the external or internal sense, or through reports of others that relate what they have arrived at of knowledge, and with what it imagines of new composite forms it invents, and with what it has of innate principles from which its measures and its inherent laws are composed, it can perform many intellectual works, among them the following works:
1 - The intellect can by itself deduce particulars from universals, and perceive universals from examining particulars, and generalize rulings through its inherent laws, or through induction.
2 - And the intellect can analogize similar things and parallels to one another.
3 - And the intellect can deduce opposites of meanings and their converses, so it perceives the contradictory and what is contained within it when it knows the contradictory, and it perceives the converse when it knows the original.
4 - And the intellect can by itself perform operations of analysis, synthesis, combination, and separation on the perceptions it has.
5 - And the intellect can by itself perceive the relations between meanings and perceptions that it has.
6 - And the intellect can by itself perceive the connections between effects and their intellectual causes, and between caused things and their logical reasons, and it can perceive the traces of things and their logical results that rely on the principle of intellectual causality, or logical reason.
7 - And the intellect can perceive from its observation of repeated experiments general rulings, even if there is no intellectual connection between the cause and the phenomenon.
8 - And when the intellect knows an imperfect thing, it can by itself ascend the ladder of perfection of that thing, recognizing its degrees degree by degree, within the laws of completion.
9 - And when the intellect perceives a perfect thing or one advanced in degrees of perfection, it can by itself descend to imagine possibilities of its deficiency degree by degree down to its lowest.
10 - And when the intellect perceives the beautiful and feels the loci of its beauty, it can by itself imagine possibilities that disfigure it and make it ugly, rendering it not beautiful.
11 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in perceiving possibilities of quantities, increasing them and decreasing them without end; increasing and decreasing quantities, increasing and decreasing spatial dimensions, increasing and decreasing temporal dimensions, increasing and decreasing measures of heat, increasing and decreasing measures of pressure, and so on for everything subject to measurement.
12 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in perceiving possibilities of variation of qualities without end, changing them and altering them without limit. If it takes a lump of malleable wax and proceeds to propose possibilities of changing its quality, its changes would not stop at a limit.
Thus it becomes clear to us how the intellect performs its endless works, within the primary materials that reach it through the external or internal sense, or that reach it through reports of others and their testimonies, added to what it has of inherent laws and what it has of imaginative ability.
But we should know that not everything the intellect perceives is of the category of certainty; rather it is divided into the divisions of perception previously explained: [truth with certainty - preponderant opinion - doubt - weak opinion (illusion) - falsehood with certainty].
And the intellect must classify its knowledge, and place each of them in its rank and degree.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological enumeration of the intellect's deductive and inferential operations. The author lists twelve abilities, each building on the previous or illustrating a different aspect of mental processing. The first ability is deduction from universals to particulars and induction from particulars to universals, highlighting the mind's capacity for generalization. The second is analogical reasoning (qiyas) among similar things. The third explains perception of opposites and converses, fundamental in logic. The fourth through sixth involve analysis, synthesis, relation perception, and causal connection. The seventh allows for generalizations from repeated experiments even without a rational causal link, indicating empirical reasoning. The eighth and ninth show the mind's ability to traverse scales from imperfection to perfection and vice versa. The tenth deals with aesthetic judgment. The eleventh and twelfth involve infinite variation of quantities and qualities, exemplified by wax. The passage concludes by acknowledging that not all intellectual products are certain; they fall into categories from certainty to falsehood, and the intellect must classify accordingly. This lesson is a structured overview of the mind's capacities, foundational for understanding the scope of logic and epistemology in Islamic thought.

Summary of the Methods of Knowledge
Author summarizes three sources of knowledge: sensory, testimony, and rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three sources of knowledge presented in the passage and explain how the intellect judges each to produce a hierarchy of certainty.

Core Idea
Knowledge flows from sense, testimony, and reason, winnowed by the intellect.

This passage condenses Islamic epistemology into three primary channels: sensory perception, testimonies of others, and the intellect's own laws. It explains how the intellect, with its innate measures, verifies these inputs and assigns them to levels ranging from certain truth to certain falsehood. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating evidence in any Islamic science, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sources
The author lists sensory perception (including inner senses and empirical generalizations), testimonies/reports of others, and the intrinsic laws of the intellect. Write down each in your own words.
Note that empirical knowledge (mujarrabāt) is included under sensory perception because it builds on sense observation plus analogical reasoning.
2
Understand the intellect's role
The intellect has innate measures that are less error-prone than sense or human testimony (since people are not infallible). It acts as an arbiter: it verifies sense perceptions and testimonies, accepts what is preponderant, discards doubts, and rejects contradictions.
3
Grasp the hierarchy
Not all inputs are equal. The hierarchy from highest to lowest: certain truth (yaqīn), preponderant opinion (ẓann rājiḥ), doubt (shakk), weak opinion (ẓann marjūḥ), and certain falsehood (bāṭil bi-yaqīn). Identify where each level fits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أردنا أن نلخص أصول طرق المعرفة تبين لنا أنها ترجع إلى الطرق التالية:
الطريق الأول: الإدراك الحسيّ بالحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة، ويدخل فيه المجربات، لأن إدراكها يعتمد على ملاحظة الحسّ، مضافاً إليها تعميم عقلي يأتي عن طريق التمثيل.
الطريق الثاني: إدراك ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف.
الطريق الثالث: إدراك قوانين العقل الذاتية، وإدراك ما يستنبطه العقل من معارف مما ورد إليه عن الطريقين السابقين.
والعقل بما لديه من موازين فطرية - هي أبعد عن الخطأ من شهادات الحسّ، وشهادات الآخرين من الناس غير المعصومين - يعتبر حكماً يرجع إليه في تمحيص المدركات بالحسّ، وتمحيص ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف، فيجزم بما يراه حقاً يقيناً، ويقبل ما يترجح لديه منها، ويطرح ما يشك به أو ينزله إلى ما دون الرجحان، ويرفض ما يحكم بامتناعه واستحالته بناءً على قوانينه الذاتية.
فليس كل وارد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك هو على مرتبة واحدة، بل هو على مراتب أعلاها الحق بيقين، وأدناها الباطل بيقين، وبينهما الظن الراجح والشك والظن المرجوح.
And if we want to summarize the principles of the methods of knowledge, it becomes clear to us that they reduce to the following methods: First method: sensory perception by the external or internal senses, and it includes empirical things (mujarrabāt), because their perception depends on sense observation, along with a mental generalization that comes through analogy (tamthīl). Second method: perception of what is contained in the testimonies and reports of others from knowledge. Third method: perception of the intrinsic laws of the intellect, and perception of what the intellect deduces from knowledge that has come to it through the previous two methods. And the intellect, with its innate measures—which are further from error than the testimonies of the senses and the testimonies of other humans who are not infallible—is considered an arbiter to which one refers in verifying sense perceptions and verifying what is contained in the testimonies and reports of others from knowledge. So it affirms what it sees as certain truth, accepts what is preponderant from them, discards what it doubts or lowers to below preponderance, and rejects what it judges to be impossible and absurd based on its intrinsic laws. So not everything that enters the perceptive intellectual apparatus is on one level; rather it is on levels: the highest is truth with certainty, the lowest is falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides a foundational classification of the sources of knowledge (طرق المعرفة) in Islamic epistemology. The author begins by stating that the principles of knowledge methods reduce to three main paths. The first is sensory perception (الإدراك الحسي), which includes both external and internal senses. Notably, the author includes empirical generalizations (المجربات) under this path, explaining that while they originate from sense observation, they also involve a mental generalization that occurs through analogical reasoning (التمثيل). The second path is knowledge derived from the testimonies and reports of others (شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم). The third path is the perception of the intellect's own intrinsic laws (قوانين العقل الذاتية) and whatever knowledge the intellect deduces from the first two sources. The author then emphasizes the role of the intellect as an arbiter: it has innate measures that are less prone to error than sensory perceptions or human testimonies (since humans are not infallible). The intellect verifies sense perceptions and testimonies, affirming what is certain, accepting what is preponderant, discarding doubts, and rejecting contradictions. The passage concludes by stating that not all inputs to the intellect are on the same level; they range from certain truth to certain falsehood, with intermediate levels of preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion. This classification is similar to the classical Islamic epistemological categories of ʿilm (certain knowledge), ẓann (opinion), and shakk (doubt).

The Epistemic Hierarchy: From Certainty to Weak Opinion
Introduces the epistemic hierarchy from certainty to weak opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to place any piece of knowledge on the epistemic spectrum from certainty (yaqīn) to weak opinion (ẓann marjūḥ) and justify its rank using the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Islamic epistemology ranks knowledge from certainty through preponderant opinion and doubt down to weak opinion, each with specific evidence conditions.

The author begins by establishing a clear hierarchy of knowledge levels. Understanding this hierarchy is essential before examining how each source of knowledge (sensory, testimonial, rational) fits into it. We will focus on the four main levels: certainty (yaqīn), preponderant opinion (ẓann rājiḥ), doubt (shakk), and weak opinion (ẓann marjūḥ).

Guided Path
1
Identify the highest level: certainty (yaqīn)
Find the conditions under which the author says knowledge becomes 'yaqīn maqṭūʿ bi-ṣidqihi' (certainty absolutely true). Note that certainty is the strongest level.
Certainty is reached when evidence is decisive and free from contradiction with rational principles.
2
Trace the descent through preponderant opinion (ẓann rājiḥ)
The author says that when the strength of perception or testimony decreases, knowledge descends to 'ẓann rājiḥ' (preponderant opinion). Recognize this as the level where evidence favours one side but is not absolute.
Ẓann rājiḥ is stronger than doubt but less than certainty.
3
Identify the middle level: doubt (shakk)
Find where the author mentions 'shakk' (doubt). It is the point where evidence is equally balanced between truth and falsehood.
Doubt is the midpoint of the scale, with no preponderance.
4
Locate the lowest level: weak opinion (ẓann marjūḥ)
The author ends with 'ẓann marjūḥ' (weak opinion). This is the weakest level before falsehood, where evidence is against the proposition.
Ẓann marjūḥ is weaker than doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف نميز بين مراتب الإدراك؟ :
١ - حين يقدم الحس شهادته المؤكدة القاطعة بعد عمليات الإدراك الحسيّ المتواترة، وتتفق معها شهادات الحواس الأخرى، وشهادات حواس الآخرين، ولا تتعارض مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه، تغدو المعرفة الحسية يقيناً مقطوعاً بصدقه، ومهما تنازلت قوة الإدراك الحسي عن هذا المستوى فإنها تتنازل رتبة المعرفة إلى مرتبة الظن الراجح بمختلف درجاته حتى مرتبة الشك، حتى آخر مرتبة الظن المرجوح.
٢ - وحين يقدم الآخرون شهاداتهم، فإن بلغت مبلغ التواتر الذي يرى العقل فيه استحالة الاتفاق على الكذب، ولا يتعارض مضمون خبرهم مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه الذاتية، تغدو المعرفة الخبرية يقيناً مقطوعاً بصدقه، ومهما تنازل الخبر عن مرتبة التواتر تنازلت رتبة المعرفة الحاصلة به إلى مرتبة الظن الراجح بمختلف درجاته حتى مرتبة الشك، ثم حتى آخر مرتبة الظن المرجوح.
ونظير الخبر المتواتر خبر النبي المعصوم المؤيد من الله بالمعجزات، ونظير الخبر المتواتر وفي قوته ما تلتقي عليه مجموعة دلائل خبرية وحسية، وقرائن ودلائل عقلية ترتقي به لدى العقل إلى مرتبة اليقين المقطوع به.
وعلى الباحث أن يتفحص الأخبار الظنية ضمن أصول البحث العلمي لدى علماء أصول الحديث (علم المصطلح).
وله أن يضيف إلى ذلك محاكماته العقلية الخاصة، المستندة إلى طرق المعرفة الأخرى.
٣ - وحين يقدم العقل أحكامه، فإن كانت خاضعة إلى أحد قوانين العقل القطعية، أو مستندة إلى ما يفيد اليقين من شهادات الحس، أو الشهادات الخبرية، كانت أحكاماً يقينية مقطوعاً بها، وإن كانت مستندة إلى ترجيح ظني بدليل من أدلة العقل كانت أحكاماً ظنية، وتناسب قوتها قوة الظن الراجح، وإن كانت أحكاماً احتمالية لا ترجيح فيها، كانت في مرتبة الشك أو في مرتبة الظن المرجوح بحسب مقدار ضعفها.
How do we distinguish between the levels of perception? 1 - When the sense presents its confirmed decisive testimony after repeated sensory perception operations, and the testimonies of other senses and the testimonies of senses of others agree with it, and it does not contradict any principle of the intellect and its laws, then sensory knowledge becomes certainty absolutely true. And to whatever extent the strength of sensory perception descends from this level, the rank of knowledge descends to the level of preponderant opinion in its various degrees until the level of doubt, and even to the last level of weak opinion. 2 - And when others present their testimonies, if they reach the level of tawātur (mass transmission) such that the intellect sees the impossibility of agreement upon falsehood, and the content of their report does not contradict any principle of the intellect and its intrinsic laws, then testimonial knowledge becomes certainty absolutely true. And to whatever extent the report descends from the level of tawātur, the rank of knowledge obtained through it descends to the level of preponderant opinion in its various degrees until the level of doubt, and then to the last level of weak opinion. And analogous to the tawātur report is the report of an infallible prophet supported by God with miracles, and analogous to the tawātur report and in its strength is that upon which a collection of testimonial and sensory evidences and rational indications converge, which elevate it in the intellect to the level of absolute certainty. And the researcher must scrutinize probable reports within the principles of scientific research according to the scholars of uṣūl al-ḥadīth (the science of terminology). And he may add to that his own rational assessments based on other methods of knowledge. 3 - And when the intellect presents its judgments, if they are subject to one of the definitive laws of the intellect, or based on what yields certainty from sensory testimonies or testimonial reports, then they are certainty judgments absolutely. And if they are based on a probable preponderance by a rational proof, then they are probable judgments, and their strength corresponds to the strength of the preponderant opinion. And if they are merely probable with no preponderance, then they are at the level of doubt or weak opinion according to the degree of weakness.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed criteria for determining the level of certainty in each of the three sources of knowledge introduced earlier. For sensory knowledge, certainty is achieved when four conditions are met: the sense presents a decisive testimony after repeated observations, other senses agree, other people's senses agree, and there is no conflict with innate rational principles. Any weakening of these conditions lowers the knowledge to preponderant opinion, doubt, or weak opinion. For testimonial knowledge, certainty is achieved when the report reaches the level of tawātur (mass transmission), which the intellect considers impossible to be false, and its content does not contradict rational principles. The author notes that similar to tawātur in strength is the report of an infallible prophet supported by miracles, or when a combination of testimonial, sensory, and rational evidences converge. He advises researchers to examine probable reports using the principles of ḥadīth criticism (muṣṭalaḥ al-ḥadīth), and also to apply their own rational reasoning. For rational judgments, certainty arises from definitive rational principles (badīhiyyāt) or from indubitable sensory/testimonial premises. If based on probable evidence, the judgment is probable; if merely possible without preponderance, it falls to doubt or weak opinion. This tripartite classification mirrors the classical Islamic epistemological hierarchy: ʿilm yaqīnī (certain knowledge), ẓann (opinion), and shakk (doubt). The author introduces a nuance: ẓann is further divided into preponderant (rājiḥ) and weak (marjūḥ), with shakk in between.

Key Terms
التواتر
الظن الراجح
الشك
الظن المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if sensory knowledge loses its decisive character, what is the first level it descends to?
A) Doubt (shakk)
B) Weak opinion (ẓann marjūḥ)
C) Preponderant opinion (ẓann rājiḥ)
D) Certainty (yaqīn)

Key Takeaway
The epistemic hierarchy of certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion provides a systematic way to evaluate knowledge strength across all sources.
